the exclamation made when an oblation is offered to the gods, is the
name of the oblation and of the wife of Agni. See below, myth 33, pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_109][109--13]].

<<page_33.html_pg33_fn16>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg33_fn16][16]]. Another
possible translation would be: ‘Then Agni arose and grew hot and became
mighty and purified.'

<<page_35.html>>

<<page_35.html_pg35_fn17>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg35_fn17][17]]. Or ‘rubbed'
-- that is, he twirled the fire sticks, his two hands, or ‘churned' as
in the previous myth.

<<page_35.html_pg35_fn18>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg35_fn18][18]]. Soma is the
ambrosia that the gods drink to make them immortal; it is an elixir,
unlike the solid food called ambrosia eaten by the Greek gods. Soma is
the expressed liquid from the Soma plant, offered during the Vedic
sacrifice, probably some kind of hallucinogenic drink; it is the name of
the moon, for the ambrosia is said to be stored in the moon; and it is
the name of the god who is the incarnation of the Soma juice.

<<page_36.html>>

<<page_36.html_pg36_fn19>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg36_fn19][19]]. Battles
between gods and demons are described below, myths 70--75, pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_271][271--300]]. Virtuous demons are perverted in
myths 62 (pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_232][232--5]]), 65 (pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_242][242--6]]), 72 (pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_274][274--80]]), and 75 (pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_290][290--300]]).

<<page_36.html_pg36_fn20>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg36_fn20][20]]. Bhisma
tells this myth to Yudhisthira.

<<page_36.html_pg36_fn21>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg36_fn21][21]]. A pun may
be intended here; Maya is the architect of the demons; the word for
illusion (derived from the same verbal root as that of Maya's name --
‘to make') is /māyā/, a feminine noun often personified as a goddess.

<<page_37.html>>

<<page_37.html_pg37_fn22>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg37_fn22][22]]. Death is
usually personified as male (Yama or simply Death -- Mṛtyu -- as a
masculine noun), but nouns ending in ‘u' may also be feminine, and
occasionally, as here, Death is female.

<<page_37.html_pg37_fn23>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg37_fn23][23]]. See below,
myth 36, p. [[#chapter4.html#page_123][123]].

<<page_38.html>>

<<page_38.html_pg38_fn24>> [[#chapter1.html#ipg38_fn24][24]]. See below,
myths [[#chapter5.html#myt52][52--5]], pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_185][185--97]].

<<page_39.html>>

<<page_39.html_pg39_fn25>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg39_fn25][25]]. Although
there are far more than thirty-three gods in the Hindu pantheon, they
are traditionally numbered at thirty-three, the ancient Vedic groups:
twelve Ādityas, eight Vasus, eleven Rudras, and two Aśvins.

<<page_39.html_pg39_fn26>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg39_fn26][26]]. There are
several different traditional ways of dividing the ‘community of
creatures'. What is probably meant here is gods, men, animals, and
inanimate objects; elsewhere, the division is into gods, demons,
ancestors, and men (see below, myth 9, p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_44][44]]) or serpents, birds, plants, and insects
(myth 10, p. [[#chapter1.html#page_47][47]]).

<<page_39.html_pg39_fn27>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg39_fn27][27]]. At the end
of each aeon or /kalpa/, the universe is burnt and flooded. See below,
myths [[#chapter4.html#myt38][38]], pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_138][138--9]], and 50--51, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_180][180--84]].

<<page_40.html>>

<<page_40.html_pg40_fn28>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg40_fn28][28]]. To join the
palms together in the /‘añjali'/ or ‘handful of flowers' gesture is to
demonstrate reverence and supplication.

<<page_40.html_pg40_fn29>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg40_fn29][29]]. Yama rules
over the dead in hell. See below, myth 19, pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_66][66--70]].

<<page_42.html>>

<<page_42.html_pg42_fn30>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg42_fn30][30]]. /Adharma/,
the violation of /dharma/, is behaviour which contradicts the natural
law of social order.

<<page_44.html>>

<<page_44.html_pg44_fn31>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg44_fn31][31]]. That is,
their status is determined by their past /karma./

<<page_45.html>>

<<page_45.html_pg45_fn32>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg45_fn32][32]]. A false
etymology based upon /gām dhayantas/, ‘speech-drinking' or ‘singing',
according to the commentator.

<<page_47.html>>

<<page_47.html_pg47_fn33>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg47_fn33][33]]. There are
said to have been fourteen different Manus each acting as progenitor in
a different era. The seventh Manu, progenitor of our present era, was
Manu the son of Vivasvat, the sun (see below, myth 19, pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_66][66--70]]).

<<page_47.html_pg47_fn34>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg47_fn34][34]]. That is,
serpents, birds, plants, and insects.

<<page_47.html_pg47_fn35>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg47_fn35][35]]. A pun may
be intended, based upon the Sanskrit verb ‘you know' (/prajānītha/) and
the noun ‘progeny' (/prajā)./

<<page_48.html>>

<<page_48.html_pg48_fn36>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg48_fn36][36]]. Here
incarnate as a god.

<<page_49.html>>

<<page_49.html_pg49_fn37>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg49_fn37][37]]. The myth of
the Kṛttikās, Ṣaṣṭhī, and Kārttikeya appears below, myth 33, pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_105][105--15]].

<<page_50.html>>

<<page_50.html_pg50_fn38>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg50_fn38][38]]. Sanaka,
Sānanda, Sanātana, and Sanatkumāra.

<<page_50.html_pg50_fn39>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg50_fn39][39]]. Or: who had
erections.

<<page_51.html>>

<<page_51.html_pg51_fn40>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg51_fn40][40]]. Dakṣa,
whose name is cognate with the Latin /dexter/, is appropriately born
from the /right/ side. The other sons preceding Dakṣa form one group of
the Seven Sages, and the four that follow (Kardama, Pañcaśikha, Voḍhu,
and Nārada) are famous Prajāpatis.

<<page_51.html_pg51_fn41>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg51_fn41][41]]. The Goose
(Haṃsa) and the Ascetic (Yati) are ancient sages.

<<page_53.html>>

<<page_53.html_pg53_fn42>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg53_fn42][42]]. See below,
myth 19, pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_66][66--70]].

<<page_54.html>>

<<page_54.html_pg54_fn43>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg54_fn43][43]]. The
al1-pervading godhead is /brahma/ (neuter). A pun may be intended with
the name of Brahmā (masculine), who here assumes a most un-metaphysical
role.

<<page_54.html_pg54_fn44>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg54_fn44][44]]. Each god
rides on an animal which is his ‘vehicle' in the metaphorical sense as
well -- his symbol, an animal in whose every instance the god is
implicitly present. Viṣņu (Hari) rides on the Garuḍa bird (see below,
myth 60, pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_222][222--3]]), Śiva on a bull (see
below, myth 69, pp. [[#chapter6.html#page_266][266--9]]), Indra on the
white elephant Airāvata (myth 69), GanĴeśa on a bandycoot (myth 69), and
the Goddess on a lion (myth 68, pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_258][258--9]]).

<<page_54.html_pg54_fn45>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg54_fn45][45]]. The
/liṅga/ or phallus is the symbol of Śiva (see below, myths
[[#chapter4.html#myt38][38--40]], pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_138][138--54]]). It is unclear whether Śiva is
here carrying this symbol or touching his own personal phallus; a pun
may be intended.

<<page_54.html_pg54_fn46>>[[#chapter1.html#ipg54_fn46][46]]. Eros, god
of desire.

<<page_58.html>>

<<page_58.html_pg58_fn1>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg58_fn1][1]]. Vṛtra is the
arch-enemy of Indra. See below, myths [[#chapter2.html#myt24][24--6]],
pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_74][74--90]].

<<page_58.html_pg58_fn2>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg58_fn2][2]]. See below,
myth 42, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_160][160--61]].

<<page_59.html>>

<<page_59.html_pg59_fn3>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg59_fn3][3]]. See below,
myths [[#chapter7.html#myt73][73--5]], pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_281][281--300]].

<<page_60.html>>

<<page_60.html_pg60_fn4>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg60_fn4][4]]. That is, the
twelve Ādityas.

<<page_60.html_pg60_fn5>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg60_fn5][5]]. See above,
myths 3--6, pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_29][29--35]], and below, myth 34,
pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_117][117--18]].

<<page_61.html>>

<<page_61.html_pg61_fn6>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg61_fn6][6]]. ‘They' refers
to the gods, according to Sāyaņa.

<<page_62.html>>

<<page_62.html_pg62_fn7>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg62_fn7][7]]. See above,
myth 3, pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_29][29--30]], and below, myths 33, pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_108][108--10]], and 43, pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_162][162--8]].

<<page_62.html_pg62_fn8>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg62_fn8][8]]. ‘He' may be
Yama, the sun, or Rudra. If it is Yama, then the ocean may be the
metaphorical ocean separating mortals from immortals, crossed by Yama in
this hymn; if ‘he' is the sun, the ocean may be the actual ocean crossed
by the sun every day; if he is Rudra, Yamī may be saying that Yama need
not fear Rudra, the punisher of incest, since he is absent across the
ocean.

<<page_63.html>>

<<page_63.html_pg63_fn9>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg63_fn9][9]]. This would
imply that his kinswoman, his sister, does not wish to become ‘alien' by
becoming his wife; an alternative translation would be: ‘that a woman of
another kind should become like him' -- that is, that an immortal woman
should become mortal like him.

<<page_63.html_pg63_fn10>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg63_fn10][10]]. The great
spirit may be Varuņa (the god who punishes sinful mortals and who sends
his thousand spies everywhere among mortals); or, as Sāyaņa suggests, he
may be Rudra, whose sons -- the Maruts -- Yama fears in spite of Yamī's
reassurance that Rudra himself is far away.

<<page_63.html_pg63_fn11>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg63_fn11][11]]. This is the
sun, born in the waters as Agni is born. The wife of the waters is
Saraņyū. Yama and Yamī are the children of Vivasvat (the sun) and
Saraņyū.

<<page_63.html_pg63_fn12>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg63_fn12][12]]. Mitra and
Varuna together represent the Indo-European duality of fire and water
and of earthly and spiritual power.

<<page_64.html>>

<<page_64.html_pg64_fn13>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg64_fn13][13]]. Or: Let
Yamī take upon herself the incest of Yama.

<<page_64.html_pg64_fn14>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg64_fn14][14]]. An
alternative interpretation of these last two verses might be: Let the
priest tend Agni day and night, as the eye of the sun dies repeatedly.
May the pair of twins, related as are heaven and earth, bear the
non-paired Agni. In later ages there will be races which will kindle the
non-paired Agni. Stretch out your arm to Agni; find another man than me
to produce the sacrificial fire.

<<page_65.html>>

<<page_65.html_pg65_fn15>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg65_fn15][15]]. See above,
myths 14d, pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_59][59--60]], and 15, p.
[[#chapter2.html#page_60][60]].

<<page_66.html>>

<<page_66.html_pg66_fn16>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg66_fn16][16]]. Compare the
impartiality of the maiden Death in myth 8, p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_42][42]].

<<page_66.html_pg66_fn17>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg66_fn17][17]]. See below,
myth 42, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_160][160--61]].

<<page_69.html>>

<<page_69.html_pg69_fn18>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg69_fn18][18]]. Here he
sings a long hymn of praise to the sun.

<<page_69.html_pg69_fn19>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg69_fn19][19]]. The lord of
the dead is Yama, who has not yet been given this office.

<<page_69.html_pg69_fn20>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg69_fn20][20]]. Skanda is
the general of the gods. See below, myth 33, pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_105][105--15]].

<<page_69.html_pg69_fn21>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg69_fn21][21]]. The word
used here for seed (/tejas/) also denotes energy; it is significant that
the sun's energy has just been compressed, made globular like a seed.

<<page_70.html>>

<<page_70.html_pg70_fn22>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg70_fn22][22]]. The
World-protectors are the guardians of the quarters, the regions or
directions of the sky: Indra, lord of the east; Yama, lord of the south;
Kubera, lord of the north; and Varuņa, lord of the west. In addition,
there are four gods of the intermediary directions: Agni (south-east),
the sun (south-west), the wind (north-west), and Soma (north-east).

<<page_72.html>>

<<page_72.html_pg72_fn23>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg72_fn23][23]]. Rasā may
here denote a river; in later Hinduism, it is the name of the
subterranean watery hell, abode of the demons. Clearly it acts, like the
Styx, to separate the other world from this world.

<<page_72.html_pg72_fn24>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg72_fn24][24]]. Pāka
(‘childish') is the name of a demon killed by Indra; the epithet may
thus mean ‘instructor of the childish' or ‘chastiser of Pāka'.

<<page_72.html_pg72_fn25>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg72_fn25][25]]. A reference
to /Ŗg Veda/ 10.108 (below, myth 23, pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_73][73--4]]).

<<page_73.html>>

<<page_73.html_pg73_fn26>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg73_fn26][26]]. The Rasā
would, apparently, be ashamed to have it known that a dog had jumped
over it.

<<page_74.html>>

<<page_74.html_pg74_fn27>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg74_fn27][27]]. Here
Bṛhaspati is an epithet, ‘lord of sacred speech', applied to Indra. By
the time of the /Bṛhaddeuatā/ version of the myth (above, myth 22, pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_72][72--3]]), Bṛhaspati and Indra had already
become separate figures. See below, myths
[[#chapter7.html#myt74][74--5]], pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_282][282--300]].

<<page_75.html>>

<<page_75.html_pg75_fn28>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg75_fn28][28]]. Or: for
Manu.

<<page_77.html>>

<<page_77.html_pg77_fn29>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg77_fn29][29]]. Śakra is a
common epithet of Indra.

<<page_78.html>>

<<page_78.html_pg78_fn30>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg78_fn30][30]]. Śacī
(‘power') is the wife of Indra.

<<page_80.html>>

<<page_80.html_pg80_fn31>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg80_fn31][31]]. Before
uttering any imprecation, one would rinse his mouth.

<<page_80.html_pg80_fn32>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg80_fn32][32]]. This is
what Tvaṣṭṛ intends to say; but by misplacing the accent he
unwittingly creates one whom Indra will overpower.

<<page_82.html>>

<<page_82.html_pg82_fn33>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg82_fn33][33]]. The myth of
the three steps is told below, myths 47--9, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_176][176--9]]; the conquest of the demons in
battle is told in myth 72, pp. [[#chapter7.html#page_277][277--80]]; and
the conquest of Bali in myth 49, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_178][178--9]].

<<page_84.html>>

<<page_84.html_pg84_fn34>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg84_fn34][34]]. Evening,
when the sun is in the western realm of Varuņa (god of morality as well
as lord of the waters) is a time when truth must be spoken, a dangerous
time for Indra to violate a treaty. It is also the time when demons
become powerful. See above, myth 9, p. [[#chapter1.html#page_44][44]].

<<page_85.html>>

<<page_85.html_pg85_fn35>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg85_fn35][35]]. Indra is
the god of rain, Wielder of the Thunderbolt.

<<page_86.html>>

<<page_86.html_pg86_fn36>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg86_fn36][36]]. See above,
myths 1, p. [[#chapter1.html#page_26][26]], and 3--4, pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_29][29--31]], and below, myth 34, pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_117][117--18]], for Prajāpati; and myth 35, pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_121][121--2]], for Śiva.

<<page_86.html_pg86_fn37>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg86_fn37][37]]. See below,
myths [[#chapter3.html#myt29][29--33]], pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_98][98--110]].

<<page_88.html>>

<<page_88.html_pg88_fn38>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg88_fn38][38]]. When a
great god thinks of anyone, that person immediately appears before him,
summoned and transported by telekinesis.

<<page_89.html>>

<<page_89.html_pg89_fn39>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg89_fn39][39]]. Or ‘let it
be diffused and scattered'. ‘Fever' here may indicate either Agni's
worry or the Fever of Brahminicide which will be diffused.

<<page_91.html>>

<<page_91.html_pg91_fn40>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg91_fn40][40]]. An
important race of demons, called Daityas, are the sons of Diti. Most of
them were slain at the time of the churning of the ocean (see below,
myth 72, pp. [[#chapter7.html#page_274][274--80]]).

<<page_92.html>>

<<page_92.html_pg92_fn41>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg92_fn41][41]]. In one
version of the myth of Indra and Ahalyā (below, myth 28, pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_94][94--6]]), Indra is branded with the marks of a
thousand female organs (/yonis/), which are later changed into eyes.

<<page_92.html_pg92_fn42>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg92_fn42][42]]. That is,
she lay with her head pointing to the north, an impure position.

<<page_93.html>>

<<page_93.html_pg93_fn43>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg93_fn43][43]]. By saying
/‘Mā rudas'./ Rudra obtains his name in the same way (below, myth 36, p.
[[#chapter4.html#page_123][123]]).

<<page_94.html>>

<<page_94.html_pg94_fn44>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg94_fn44][44]]. See above,
myth 13, pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_56][56--7]].

<<page_94.html_pg94_fn45>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg94_fn45][45]]. It is the
duty of a Hindu to impregnate his wife during her fertile season, and
forest-dwellers who live with their wives are not supposed to have
intercourse with them at any other time.

<<page_96.html>>

<<page_96.html_pg96_fn46>>[[#chapter2.html#ipg96_fn46][46]]. Indra
prides himself on having performed a hundred horse sacrifices, both in
demonstration of his royal nature and to expiate his many sins.

<<page_97.html>>

<<page_97.html_pg97_fn1>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg97_fn1][1]]. See above,
myth 26, pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_87][87--90]].

<<page_97.html_pg97_fn2>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg97_fn2][2]]. See below,
myth 73, p. [[#chapter7.html#page_281][281]].

<<page_97.html_pg97_fn3>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg97_fn3][3]]. See below,
myth 43, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_166][166--8]].

<<page_98.html>>

<<page_98.html_pg98_fn4>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg98_fn4][4]]. Agni assumes
various forms in the sky (the sun), in the air (lightning, comets), and
on earth (sacrificial fire, domestic fire, and the digestive fire within
all men).

<<page_98.html_pg98_fn5>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg98_fn5][5]]. The Hotṛ is
the priest who recites the /Ŗg Veda/ at a sacrifice.

<<page_98.html_pg98_fn6>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg98_fn6][6]]. Or: a man.

<<page_99.html>>

<<page_99.html_pg99_fn7>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg99_fn7][7]]. Or: Make the
clarified butter the share allotted to the waters, and make the
sacrificial victim the share allotted to the plants.

<<page_99.html_pg99_fn8>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg99_fn8][8]]. ‘Vaṣaṭ' is
the exclamation used when making an offering of an oblation to a deity
(in contrast with ‘Svāhā', used in making an oblation to all of the gods
together). See myths 5, pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_32][32--3]], and 33,
pp. [[#chapter3.html#page_109][109--13]].

<<page_100.html>>

<<page_100.html_pg100_fn9>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg100_fn9][9]]. This may
mean that he removed his bodies from their hiding places.

<<page_100.html_pg100_fn10>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg100_fn10][10]]. The
fragrant resin is /guggulu/, or bdellium, used for perfume or medicine;
/tejana/ is a kind of bamboo; /avakā/ is a grass growing in marshy land.
/Kāśa (Saccharum spontaneum/) is used for mats and roofs; /kuśa/ grass
(/Poa cynosuroides/) is a long, pointed grass used in sacrificial
rituals. Both Kāśa and Kuśa are personified as servants of Yama.

<<page_101.html>>

<<page_101.html_pg101_fn11>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg101_fn11][11]]. See
below, myth 43, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_164][164--8]].

<<page_102.html>>

<<page_102.html_pg102_fn12>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg102_fn12][12]]. Agni had
hidden within water or within the elemental fire.

<<page_102.html_pg102_fn13>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg102_fn13][13]]. /Rasa/
can denote either ‘tongue' or ‘taste'.

<<page_103.html>>

<<page_103.html_pg103_fn14>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg103_fn14][14]]. The
/aśvattha/ is the sacred fig tree.

<<page_103.html_pg103_fn15>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg103_fn15][15]]. The
/śamī/ is the tree from which fire-sticks are made; the tree itself is
said to contain fire in its interior (literally its womb [/garbha/] or
perhaps its hollow), and this fire is kindled from the sticks.

<<page_104.html>>

<<page_104.html_pg104_fn16>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg104_fn16][16]]. At this
point the story of the birth of Skanda is narrated in somewhat different
terms from the version which I have translated in myth 33, pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_108][108--10]].

<<page_104.html_pg104_fn17>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg104_fn17][17]]. See
below, myth 68, pp. [[#chapter6.html#page_252][252--9]].

<<page_104.html_pg104_fn18>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg104_fn18][18]]. See
above, myth 25, pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_83][83--4]].

<<page_105.html>>

<<page_105.html_pg105_fn19>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg105_fn19][19]]. See
below, myth 73, p. [[#chapter7.html#page_281][281]].

<<page_105.html_pg105_fn20>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg105_fn20][20]]. See
below, myths [[#chapter4.html#myt35][35--6]], pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_119][119--25]].

<<page_106.html>>

<<page_106.html_pg106_fn21>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg106_fn21][21]]. Keśin
(‘Long-haired') is the name of a demon here. In the /Ŗg Veda/, Keśin is
a long-haired sage who drinks poison with Rudra; in the Purāņas, Keśin
is the name of a wild horse demon killed by Kṛṣņa.

<<page_106.html_pg106_fn22>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg106_fn22][22]]. That is,
whose wife or whose daughter.

<<page_107.html>>

<<page_107.html_pg107_fn23>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg107_fn23][23]]. The sun
is said to rise from behind the Hill of Sunrise in the east and set
behind the Hill of Sunset in the west; it rises out of the eastern ocean
and sets into the western ocean.

<<page_107.html_pg107_fn24>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg107_fn24][24]]. The
/parvans/ are the days of full moon and new moon each month.

<<page_107.html_pg107_fn25>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg107_fn25][25]]. The
Indians see a hare in the moon where Europeans see a man.

<<page_110.html>>

<<page_110.html_pg110_fn26>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg110_fn26][26]]. The verb
used here may also refer to the kindling of a fire.

<<page_110.html_pg110_fn27>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg110_fn27][27]]. Guha,
‘Reared in a secret place', is a name of Skanda.

<<page_111.html>>

<<page_111.html_pg111_fn28>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg111_fn28][28]]. See
below, myth 37, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_133][133--7]].

<<page_113.html>>

<<page_113.html_pg113_fn29>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg113_fn29][29]]. Or: for
the goddess Śakti.

<<page_114.html>>

<<page_114.html_pg114_fn30>>[[#chapter3.html#ipg114_fn30][30]]. The
Mother born of anger, drinker of blood, is Kālī.

<<page_116.html>>

<<page_116.html_pg116_fn1>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg116_fn1][1]]. See above,
myths 1, p. [[#chapter1.html#page_26][26]], 3, pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_29][29--31]], and 4, p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_31][31]], for the myth of Prajāpati; below, myth

<<page_117.html>>

<<page_117.html_pg117_fn2>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg117_fn2][2]]. See below,
myth 36, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_123][123--5]], for Bhaga and Pūṣan,
and myth 43, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_164][164--8]], for Śiva's seed.

<<page_117.html_pg117_fn3>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg117_fn3][3]]. Or: half of
his seed was shed. (In some versions of the Skanda myth, Agni sheds half
of his seed in his wife and the other half elsewhere.)

<<page_117.html_pg117_fn4>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg117_fn4][4]]. A reference
to /Ŗg Veda/ 10.61.7. See above, myth 1, p.

<<page_117.html_pg117_fn5>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg117_fn5][5]]. ‘This' may

the arrow itself, but in the context of other variants of the myth,
‘this' probably refers to the seed.

<<page_118.html>>

<<page_118.html_pg118_fn6>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg118_fn6][6]]. See above,
myth 3, pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_30][30--31]].

<<page_118.html_pg118_fn7>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg118_fn7][7]]. See above,
myths 25--6, pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_77][77--90]]. Both Indra and
Rudra/Śiva are beheaders and parricides, and both are punished by the
Fury of Brah-minicide.

<<page_119.html>>

<<page_119.html_pg119_fn8>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg119_fn8][8]]. In most
versions of this myth, Dakṣa's daughter Satī, Śiva's wife, drives Śiva
to destroy her father's sacrifice (see below, myth 67, pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_250][250--51]]). Here, the woman is Pārvatī,
Śiva's second wife, daughter of the mountain Himālaya (see below, myth
41, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_157][157--9]]).

<<page_121.html>>

<<page_121.html_pg121_fn9>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg121_fn9][9]]. Men
condemned to death, as well as Buddhists and other heretics, wore red
clothes.

<<page_122.html>>

<<page_122.html_pg122_fn10>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg122_fn10][10]]. See
above, myth 25, p. [[#chapter2.html#page_81][81]].

<<page_123.html>>

<<page_123.html_pg123_fn11>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg123_fn11][11]]. See
above, myth 28, pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_94][94--6]]. Kratu is
identified with the castrated Śiva in myth 39, pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_143][143--5]]. See also below, myth 38, pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_139][139--41]].

<<page_123.html_pg123_fn12>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg123_fn12][12]]. Compare
the derivation of the Maruts' name, above, myth 27, p.
[[#chapter2.html#page_93][93]].

<<page_123.html_pg123_fn13>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg123_fn13][13]]. Satī,
the daughter just created by Dakṣa.

<<page_124.html>>

<<page_124.html_pg124_fn14>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg124_fn14][14]]. As Kratu
is the sacrifice, he here replaces Prajāpati, for whose act of incest
Kratu's punishment (castration) is set forth in ancient Indian law.

<<page_125.html>>

<<page_125.html_pg125_fn15>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg125_fn15][15]].
Literally, his fruit.

<<page_125.html_pg125_fn16>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg125_fn16][16]]. See
above, myth 2, pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_27][27--8]].

<<page_125.html_pg125_fn17>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg125_fn17][17]]. See
above, myth 9, pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_44][44--6]].

<<page_126.html>>

<<page_126.html_pg126_fn18>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg126_fn18][18]]. See
below, myths [[#chapter7.html#myt73][73--5]], pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_274][274--300]].

<<page_126.html_pg126_fn19>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg126_fn19][19]]. In order
to destroy Tāraka, the gods caused Kāma to excite Śiva (see below, myth
41, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_154][154--6]]) so that Skanda would be
born to kill Tāraka (myth 43, p. [[#chapter4.html#page_163][163]]).

<<page_127.html>>

<<page_127.html_pg127_fn20>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg127_fn20][20]]. The
names mean ‘Star-eyed', ‘Lotus-eyed', and ‘Garlanded-with-lightning'.

<<page_131.html>>

<<page_131.html_pg131_fn21>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg131_fn21][21]]. The
Sanskrit word for dynasty (/vaṃśa/) has the primary meaning of a bamboo
reed; perhaps a pun is intended, if the shaft is made of bamboo.
Moreover, as the primary meaning of /yuga/ is a yoke, another pun may be
intended.

<<page_132.html>>

<<page_132.html_pg132_fn22>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg132_fn22][22]]. /Dharma/
(social law), /artha/ (success or wealth), and /kāma/ (sensual pleasure)
formed the triad of goals in ancient India, to which a fourth goal --
/mokṣa/, release -- was later added.

<<page_132.html_pg132_fn23>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg132_fn23][23]]. The two
clouds are named Saṃvartaka (‘Dissolution', also a name of the
submarine doomsday mare) and Balāhaka (‘the Crane'). Elsewhere, five
more doomsday clouds are enumerated (see below, myth 51, p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_183][183]]).

<<page_132.html_pg132_fn24>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg132_fn24][24]]. A skin
would be stretched over the frame of the chariot.

<<page_132.html_pg132_fn25>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg132_fn25][25]]. The
Lokapālas: Indra ruler of the gods. Varuņa ruler of waters, Yama king of
the dead, and Kubera lord of wealth.

<<page_133.html>>

<<page_133.html_pg133_fn26>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg133_fn26][26]]. The
Atharvan is the priest presiding over fire and Soma. Dadhyañc is the son
of an Atharvan priest (see above, myths 13, pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_56][56--7]], and 14, pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_58][58--9]]).

<<page_133.html_pg133_fn27>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg133_fn27][27]]. The term
/Itihāsa/ (literally, ‘thus he spoke') refers to historical tradition,
dynastic histories and legends, in contrast with sacred tradition.

<<page_136.html>>

<<page_136.html_pg136_fn28>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg136_fn28][28]]. A name
of Viṣņu. See below, myths [[#chapter5.html#myt54][54]], p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_186][186]], and 72, pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_274][274--80]].

<<page_137.html>>

<<page_137.html_pg137_fn29>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg137_fn29][29]]. As the
sun sets in the western ocean, this is an appropriate place to dispose
of the demon cities (or the universe itself) when they meet their fiery
doom.

<<page_137.html_pg137_fn30>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg137_fn30][30]]. See
above, myth 36, p. [[#chapter4.html#page_123][123]].

<<page_138.html>>

<<page_138.html_pg138_fn31>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg138_fn31][31]]. This
dwelling, according to the commentator, is the subterranean watery hell,
which is also the dwelling place of the demons.

<<page_139.html>>

<<page_139.html_pg139_fn32>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg139_fn32][32]]. Ka, a
name of Brahmā, also means ‘Who?'. An agnostic Vedic verse (10. 129.6)
which asked, ‘Who [/ka/] knows whence this creation was born?' was later
interpreted as a statement: ‘Who [the god Who?, Prajāpati] knows whence
this creation was born.'

<<page_139.html_pg139_fn33>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg139_fn33][33]]. The
creative power of the god, often personified as a goddess.

<<page_139.html_pg139_fn34>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg139_fn34][34]]. An
alternative reading (/ucchādya/ for /ucchidya/) would be translated,
‘uncovering my own seed'.

<<page_140.html>>

<<page_140.html_pg140_fn35>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg140_fn35][35]]. Past,
present, and future are the three times; the /liṅga/ is their image.

<<page_140.html_pg140_fn36>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg140_fn36][36]]. Down to
the subterranean hell.

<<page_140.html_pg140_fn37>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg140_fn37][37]]. Viṣņu
assumes the form of a boar and Brahmā the form of a goose.

<<page_141.html>>

<<page_141.html_pg141_fn38>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg141_fn38][38]]. See
above, myth 33, pp. [[#chapter3.html#page_108][108--12]].

<<page_143.html>>

<<page_143.html_pg143_fn39>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg143_fn39][39]]. Bhṛgu's
curse is described in myth 75, p. [[#chapter7.html#page_293][293]],
below, Gautama's in myth 28, p. [[#chapter2.html#page_95][95]], above.

<<page_143.html_pg143_fn40>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg143_fn40][40]]. For the
myths of Nahuṣa, the Vasus, and Māņḍavya, see the glossary.

<<page_143.html_pg143_fn41>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg143_fn41][41]]. A
reference to the four stages of life (/āśramas/): chaste student,
householder, forest-dweller, and ascetic. Although the sages refer to
themselves as householders, they practise extreme asceticism.

<<page_143.html_pg143_fn42>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg143_fn42][42]]. The
loincloth of the ascetic.

<<page_144.html>>

<<page_144.html_pg144_fn43>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg144_fn43][43]]. The
three upper classes (priests, nobles, and commoners) are technically
twice-born, as they are ‘reborn' at the time of their consecration; but
‘twice-born' usually denotes only the brahmin class.

<<page_144.html_pg144_fn44>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg144_fn44][44]]. In this,
the sages follow the practice of yet another stage of life, that of the
forest-dwellers, combining the roles of householders and ascetics.

<<page_144.html_pg144_fn45>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg144_fn45][45]]. This
term is used to denote a position of sexual intercourse in which the
woman is on top of the man, but it may here imply nothing more specific
than general unchastity or perversions. The words of the sages are
contradictory and vague, betraying their lack of control or
understanding: they say that Śiva seduced their daughters, though in
fact it was their wives who followed him; they say that they castrated
him, though he himself did this, allowing them to think that this
happened as the result of their curse upon him.

<<page_145.html>>

<<page_145.html_pg145_fn46>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg145_fn46][46]]. The
Śrivatsa is the twist of hair on Viṣņu's chest, and the discus is
Viṣņu's weapon. Thus Śiva is here identified with Viṣņu.

<<page_145.html_pg145_fn47>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg145_fn47][47]].
Dharmaketu (‘Having the law as his banner') is an epithet of the Buddha.
Again Śiva assumes Viṣņu's role and becomes the Buddha in the Kali Age;
see myth 62, pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_232][232--5]].

<<page_145.html_pg145_fn48>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg145_fn48][48]]. A
reference to the three strands or /gunas/; see myth 9, p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_44][44]].

<<page_145.html_pg145_fn49>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg145_fn49][49]]. ‘Clothed
in the sky', that is, naked; a term applied to Śaiva or Jain beggars.

<<page_146.html>>

<<page_146.html_pg146_fn50>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg146_fn50][50]]. It was a
sign of reverence to walk around someone to the right, clockwise.

<<page_147.html>>

<<page_147.html_pg147_fn51>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg147_fn51][51]]. The
/liṅga./

<<page_147.html_pg147_fn52>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg147_fn52][52]]. The law
of the four classes and four stages of life.

<<page_147.html_pg147_fn53>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg147_fn53][53]].
Literally, against the hair. A reference to unorthodox, perhaps Tantric,
rites.

<<page_147.html_pg147_fn54>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg147_fn54][54]]. That is,
made (kindled) but not completed (consumed), often said of gold.

<<page_148.html>>

<<page_148.html_pg148_fn55>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg148_fn55][55]]. That is,
they are fires and drink fire, while the gods are Soma-drinkers.

<<page_148.html_pg148_fn56>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg148_fn56][56]].
According to Sāṅkhya philosophy, Man is inert spirit until he unites
with the active female element, Nature (/prakṛti/), which is composed
of the three strands or qualities.

<<page_148.html_pg148_fn57>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg148_fn57][57]]. A
reference to the Kāpālikas or skull-bearers, who wander about holding a
human skull in their hands, in imitation of Śiva's act of beheading
Brahmā and carrying Brahmā's skull to Benares.

<<page_149.html>>

<<page_149.html_pg149_fn58>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg149_fn58][58]]. See
above, myth 12, pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_54][54--5]].

<<page_149.html_pg149_fn59>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg149_fn59][59]]. Manu
Svāyambhūva, the first Manu, progenitor of mankind in the Kṛta Age.

<<page_150.html>>

<<page_150.html_pg150_fn60>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg150_fn60][60]]. Viṣņu
is here said to embody all of the third strand or quality, while Brahmā
and Śiva are made of passion and darkness. Compare the Śaiva attitude,
wherein Śiva embodies all three strands, though his antecedent Agni
predominates in the quality of darkness, while Brahmā and Viṣņu (‘forms
of Rudra') are still characterized by passion and goodness (see above,
myth 39, p. [[#chapter4.html#page_145][145]]), and myth 10, p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_50][50]].

<<page_151.html>>

<<page_151.html_pg151_fn61>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg151_fn61][61]]. The
symbol of the female organ of generation, particularly that of the
Goddess, worshipped in union with the /liṅga./

<<page_153.html>>

<<page_153.html_pg153_fn62>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg153_fn62][62]]. Viṣņu
sleeps on Ananta, the serpent of eternity.

<<page_156.html>>

<<page_156.html_pg156_fn63>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg156_fn63][63]]. A name
of Viṣņu, ‘Descendant of Madhu' or ‘Vernal'.

<<page_156.html_pg156_fn64>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg156_fn64][64]]. There is
irony in this boast, for the irascible Durvāsas is an incarnation of
Śiva -- whom Kāma is about to attack in vain.

<<page_157.html>>

<<page_157.html_pg157_fn65>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg157_fn65][65]]. A name
of Nandin, guardian of Śiva's door. See below, myth 68, pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_254][254--61]].

<<page_157.html_pg157_fn66>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg157_fn66][66]]. ‘The
Maddener', a name of Kāma.

<<page_159.html>>

<<page_159.html_pg159_fn67>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg159_fn67][67]]. The
Black Goddess (or goddess of doomsday). See below, myth 68, p.
[[#chapter6.html#page_259][259]].

<<page_159.html_pg159_fn68>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg159_fn68][68]].
‘Memory', an epithet of Kāma. His five arrows are made of the sun lotus,
the /aśoka/ flower, the mango, jasmine, and blue lotus, and they cause
infatuation, excitement, parching or withering, heating, and paralysis
(or stiffening).

<<page_160.html>>

<<page_160.html_pg160_fn69>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg160_fn69][69]]. /Saumya/
here could mean either ‘gentle' or actually made of ambrosia.

<<page_161.html>>

<<page_161.html_pg161_fn70>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg161_fn70][70]]. That is,
so that it does not devour all the water and get loose again.

<<page_162.html>>

<<page_162.html_pg162_fn71>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg162_fn71][71]]. Compare
their role in the other version of Skanda's birth, above, myth 33, pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_108][108--12]].

<<page_162.html_pg162_fn72>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg162_fn72][72]]. The
earth is said to rest upon a great tortoise; this became one of the
avatars of Viṣņu. See below, myth 72, p.
[[#chapter7.html#page_275][275]].

<<page_164.html>>

<<page_164.html_pg164_fn73>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg164_fn73][73]]. Śivā,
the wife of Śiva, is an epithet of Pārvatī.

<<page_165.html>>

<<page_165.html_pg165_fn74>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg165_fn74][74]]. See
below, myth 69, pp. [[#chapter6.html#page_262][262--9]].

<<page_166.html>>

<<page_166.html_pg166_fn75>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg166_fn75][75]]. Or
energy (/tejas/).

<<page_168.html>>

<<page_168.html_pg168_fn76>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg168_fn76][76]]. See
above, myth 3, pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_29][29--30]].

<<page_168.html_pg168_fn77>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg168_fn77][77]]. See
above, myth 33, pp. [[#chapter3.html#page_105][105--15]], and below,
myth 69, pp. [[#chapter6.html#page_262][262--9]].

<<page_169.html>>

<<page_169.html_pg169_fn78>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg169_fn78][78]]. See
above, myths 3--6, pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_29][29--35]], and 34, pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_117][117--18]].

<<page_169.html_pg169_fn79>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg169_fn79][79]]. The
commentator notes that darkness falls because Śiva's eyes are the moon,
the sun, and fire.

<<page_169.html_pg169_fn80>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg169_fn80][80]]. The
commentator points out that the drop is a drop of sweat, and that it is
heated by the fire of the third eye in Śiva's forehead.

<<page_169.html_pg169_fn81>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg169_fn81][81]]. ‘The
golden one', an epithet of Pārvatī. See below, myth 68, pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_252][252--9]].

<<page_170.html>>

<<page_170.html_pg170_fn82>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg170_fn82][82]].
Hiraņyakaśipu, ‘Golden-garments', twin brother of Hiraņyākṣā, who
persecuted his son Prahlāda for worshipping Viṣņu; Viṣņu then assumed
his Man-lion incarnation and killed Hiraņyakṣśipu.

<<page_171.html>>

<<page_171.html_pg171_fn83>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg171_fn83][83]]. Manu
(9.158--160) enumerates twelve kinds of sons, only the first six kinsmen
and legitimate heirs: the legitimate son of the body, the son begotten
on a wife, the son adopted, the son made, the son secretly born, and the
son cast off; the son of an unmarried girl, the son begotten on a
remarried woman, the son of a servant woman, the son received with the
wife (that is, born of a woman already pregnant at her marriage), the
son bought, and the son who gives himself. The commentator on this myth
considers the last three and the adopted son to be subsumed under one
heading and disregarded as ‘given' rather than ‘begotten'.

<<page_172.html>>

<<page_172.html_pg172_fn84>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg172_fn84][84]].
‘Golden-eye', another name of Hiraņyākṣa.

<<page_173.html>>

<<page_173.html_pg173_fn85>>[[#chapter4.html#ipg173_fn85][85]]. See
above, myth 43, p. [[#chapter4.html#page_162][162]], for the dance, and
myth 39, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_147][147--9]], for the symbolism of
ashes.

<<page_175.html>>

<<page_175.html_pg175_fn1>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg175_fn1][1]]. Viṣņu is
generally said to have had ten incarnations: the fish (myth 51, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_181][181--4]]), the tortoise (myth 72, p.
[[#chapter7.html#page_275][275]]), the boar (myth 54, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_186][186--7]]), the Man-lion (myth 55, p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_195][195]]). the dwarf (myth 48, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_177][177--8]]), Rama (myth 56, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_198][198--204]]), Kṛṣņa (myth 57, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_206][206--13]]), the Buddha (myth 62, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_232][232--5]]), and Kalkin (myth 63, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_236][236--7]]), as well as Paraśurāma (‘Rāma with
the axe'), the son of Jamadagni, who beheaded his unchaste mother and
killed all the Kśatriyas.

<<page_176.html>>

<<page_176.html_pg176_fn2>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg176_fn2][2]]. See above,
myth 12, pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_54][54--5]], for the worship of
Viṣņu's feet, and below, myth 60, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_224][224--5]], for the footprint.

<<page_177.html>>

<<page_177.html_pg177_fn3>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg177_fn3][3]]. See above,
myth 26, p. [[#chapter2.html#page_88][88]], for Indra, and myths 29--32,
pp. [[#chapter3.html#page_98][98--104]], for Agni. The soul in
transmigration was said to ‘hide' temporarily in plants and in water.

<<page_177.html_pg177_fn4>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg177_fn4][4]]. See above,
myth 36, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_123][123--5]].

<<page_177.html_pg177_fn5>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg177_fn5][5]]. Compare the
simultaneously minute and enormous forms of Krana, below, myth 59, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_220][220--21]].

<<page_178.html>>

<<page_178.html_pg178_fn6>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg178_fn6][6]]. That is,
they made Agni the rising sun.

<<page_180.html>>

<<page_180.html_pg180_fn7>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg180_fn7][7]]. ‘Fish
swallows fish' was the basis of the Indian term for anarchy, the
equivalent of our ‘dog eats dog'.

<<page_180.html_pg180_fn8>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg180_fn8][8]]. The
/jhaṣa/, a large fish of indefinite species, is the constellation
Pisces.

<<page_181.html>>

<<page_181.html_pg181_fn9>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg181_fn9][9]]. See below,
myth 59, pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_220][220--21]].

<<page_182.html>>

<<page_182.html_pg182_fn10>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg182_fn10][10]]. First a
bright little fish found in shallow water; later a large carp that preys
on other fish, an interesting development in the light of the myth.

<<page_183.html>>

<<page_183.html_pg183_fn11>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg183_fn11][11]]. The
mare's mouth is tightened to keep the fire inside her.

<<page_186.html>>

<<page_186.html_pg186_fn12>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg186_fn12][12]]. ‘Having
the waters for his abode', a quotation from Manu 1.8.

<<page_187.html>>

<<page_187.html_pg187_fn13>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg187_fn13][13]]. Earth,
aether, heaven, and the underworld.

<<page_188.html>>

<<page_188.html_pg188_fn14>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg188_fn14][14]]. Compare
the blood sacrifice of the buffalo, below, myth /66./ pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_248][248--9]].

<<page_189.html>>

<<page_189.html_pg189_fn15>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg189_fn15][15]]. In early
versions of this myth, Viṣņu begets in the earth one son, Naraka
(‘Little Man') or Bhaumya (‘Born of Earth'), a troublesome demon. Here
Naraka remains an embryo, but his mischief is predicted.

<<page_189.html_pg189_fn16>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg189_fn16][16]].
‘Well-rounded', ‘Golden', and ‘Horrible'.

<<page_190.html>>

<<page_190.html_pg190_fn17>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg190_fn17][17]].
Wishing-trees produce all things desired, on their branches, as
wishing-cows are ‘milked' of what one wants.

<<page_193.html>>

<<page_193.html_pg193_fn18>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg193_fn18][18]]. The
fourth of the seven worlds above the earth.

<<page_194.html>>

<<page_194.html_pg194_fn19>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg194_fn19][19]]. The
constellations are floating palaces drawn by chariots.

<<page_195.html>>

<<page_195.html_pg195_fn20>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg195_fn20][20]].
/Cyprinus rohitaka./ Compare the earlier versions of Viṣņu's fish
incarnation, above, myths 50--51, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_180][180--184]].

<<page_195.html_pg195_fn21>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg195_fn21][21]]. In
actual historical development, mirrored in the time sequence of the
myth, Viṣņu's Man-lion incarnation preceded, and perhaps inspired,
Śiva's /śarabha/ form. The way in which the Man-lion appears, emerging
from within a stone pillar, to save his devotee from a murderous demon
may also have inspired the famous image of Śiva appearing from within
the stone /liṅga/ to save his devotee from the god of death.

<<page_196.html>>

<<page_196.html_pg196_fn22>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg196_fn22][22]]. See
above, myth 33, pp. [[#chapter3.html#page_108][108--12]].

<<page_197.html>>

<<page_197.html_pg197_fn23>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg197_fn23][23]]. A sacred
syllable indicating assent and approval, like ‘Amen'.

<<page_198.html>>

<<page_198.html_pg198_fn24>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg198_fn24][24]]. Sītā
(‘the Furrow') is said to have been born out of a furrow in a field
ploughed by Janaka, king of Videha. As Mithilā is the capital of Videha,
Sītā is known as Maithilī.

<<page_199.html>>

<<page_199.html_pg199_fn25>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg199_fn25][25]]. Agastya
dwelt in a hermitage south of the Vindhya mountains' and was chief of
the sages of the south, using his ascetic powers to conquer and control
the Rākṣasas who infested the south.

<<page_201.html>>

<<page_201.html_pg201_fn26>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg201_fn26][26]]. ‘To take
a woman's hand' (in the marriage ritual) signifies ‘to marry'.

<<page_202.html>>

<<page_202.html_pg202_fn27>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg202_fn27][27]]. Conch
shell, discus, mace, and lotus, held in his four hands.

<<page_203.html>>

<<page_203.html_pg203_fn28>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg203_fn28][28]]. The sun
at dawn.

<<page_203.html_pg203_fn29>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg203_fn29][29]]. Certain
signs are said to distinguish gods from mortals, with whom they may be
otherwise identical: gods do not blink, their garlands do not wither,
they do not perspire, no dust settles on them, and their feet do not
quite touch the ground.

<<page_205.html>>

<<page_205.html_pg205_fn30>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg205_fn30][30]]. See
above, myths 27, pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_91][91--4]], and 33, pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_108][108--12]].

<<page_206.html>>

<<page_206.html_pg206_fn32>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg206_fn32][32]]. See

<<page_206.html_pg206_fn33>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg206_fn33][33]]. See

<<page_207.html>>

<<page_207.html_pg207_fn34>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg207_fn34][34]]. Kaṃsa
refers to the prophecy that he will be killed by Devakī's son.

<<page_207.html_pg207_fn35>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg207_fn35][35]]. Kālanemi
was a great demon, son of Virocana, grandson of Hiraņyakaśipu. He was
killed by Viṣņu but became incarnate again in Kaṃsa and in Kāliya. As
Kaṃsa is thus identified with the father of the embryos who become
reborn as Balarāma and Kṛṣņa, he is himself in a sense the father of
Kṛṣņa.

<<page_208.html>>

<<page_208.html_pg208_fn36>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg208_fn36][36]].
Hiraņyakaśipu is the great-grandfather of the Six Embryos.

<<page_208.html_pg208_fn37>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg208_fn37][37]]. That is,
Kālanemi divided them and Kālanemi's reincarnation, Kaṃsa, will kill
them.

<<page_208.html_pg208_fn38>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg208_fn38][38]]. That is,
when they are dead.

<<page_209.html>>

<<page_209.html_pg209_fn39>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg209_fn39][39]]. ‘He who
draws or ploughs', an epithet of Balarāma, brother of Kṛṣņa.

<<page_209.html_pg209_fn40>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg209_fn40][40]]. When the
embryo is taken from Devakī's body to be placed in Rohiņī, it is thought
that Devakī has miscarried.

<<page_210.html>>

<<page_210.html_pg210_fn41>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg210_fn41][41]]. See
below, myth 68, p. [[#chapter6.html#page_259][259]], for another
explanation of this name.

<<page_210.html_pg210_fn42>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg210_fn42][42]]. Her
‘residences' are the shrines dedicated to Kālī

<<page_211.html>>

<<page_211.html_pg211_fn43>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg211_fn43][43]].
Twenty-seven of Dakṣa's daughters were given in marriage to Soma, the
moon, and are regarded as the twenty-seven lunar asterisms. The fourth,
Rohiņī, was favoured by Soma to the exclusion of all the others until
Dakṣa cursed Soma to be childless and to suffer from consumption (the
cause of the moon's waning). Soma's wives interceded on his behalf, and
Dakṣa modified his curse so that the waning would be periodical.

<<page_213.html>>

<<page_213.html_pg213_fn44>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg213_fn44][44]]. This
verse depends upon a play on words. /Payodhara/, ‘liquid-bearing', may
refer either to clouds (rain-bearing) or breasts (milk-bearing).

<<page_214.html>>

<<page_214.html_pg214_fn45>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg214_fn45][45]]. For
Saramā, see myth 22, pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_72][72--3]]; for Skanda,
myth 33, p. [[#chapter3.html#page_114][114]]; for the churning, myth 72,
p. [[#chapter7.html#page_277][277]].

<<page_215.html>>

<<page_215.html_pg215_fn46>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg215_fn46][46]]. In the
/Harivaṃśa/, Pūtanā is a bird, who defies the distinction between the
species of aves and mammals by giving Kṛṣņa her poisonous breast to
suck.

<<page_215.html_pg215_fn47>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg215_fn47][47]]. Śrī,
‘Prosperity', is Lakṣmī, the wife of Viṣņu and thus the wife of
Kṛṣņa. She carries a lotus, as Pūtanā does here.

<<page_216.html>>

<<page_216.html_pg216_fn48>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg216_fn48][48]]. Here
they chant a series of spells protecting him from various ogresses and
diseases.

<<page_217.html>>

<<page_217.html_pg217_fn49>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg217_fn49][49]]. Vasudeva
was called Anakadundubhi because at his birth the gods, foreseeing the
birth of Kṛṣņa, caused drums (/anaka/ and /dundubhi/) to resound in
heaven.

<<page_218.html>>

<<page_218.html_pg218_fn50>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg218_fn50][50]]. See
/Mahābhārata/ 3.183--190 and /Matsya/ 167.

pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_44][44--6]].

<<page_218.html_pg218_fn53>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg218_fn53][53]]. Rāma in
the /Bhāgavata Purāņa/ stories of Kṛṣņa always refers to Balarāma,
Saṃkarṣaṇa, the brother of Kṛṣņa, and has nothing to do with the
Rāma of the /Rāmāyaņa./

<<page_218.html_pg218_fn54>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg218_fn54][54]]. The
commentator says that this is the sound of their own anklets and
bangles.

<<page_221.html>>

<<page_221.html_pg221_fn55>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg221_fn55][55]]. See

<<page_222.html>>

<<page_222.html_pg222_fn56>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg222_fn56][56]]. ‘He who
has beautiful feathers', an epithet of Garuḍa. Garuḍa is also called
Tārkṣya, an epithet originally designating a horse.

<<page_222.html_pg222_fn57>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg222_fn57][57]]. Kadrū,
daughter of Dakṣa, wife of Kaśyapa, was the progenitor of the race of
/nāgas/ or snakes. She once made a bet with Vinatā, mother of Garuḍa
and the birds, that the horse Uccaiḥśravas had a black tail. Winning
this bet by a ruse, she made Vinatā her slave, promising her freedom
only if Garuḍa brought the ambrosia to the snakes. Garuḍa stole the
ambrosia from the gods and placed it before the snakes on a bed of sharp
/kuśa/ grass. The snakes freed Vinatā and licked the grass, making their
tongues forked, but Indra then stole back the ambrosia.

<<page_226.html>>

<<page_226.html_pg226_fn58>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg226_fn58][58]]. Great
cobras are said to have rubies imbedded in their hoods.

<<page_227.html>>

<<page_227.html_pg227_fn59>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg227_fn59][59]]. That is,
he is in a form from which your anger will soon release him, or a form
which justifies your anger.

<<page_230.html>>

<<page_230.html_pg230_fn60>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg230_fn60][60]]. Because
they had exposed their naked bodies to Varuņa, god of the waters.
Compare the sin of the Kṛttikās in exposing themselves to Agni by
bathing naked, above, myth 43, p. [[#chapter4.html#page_167][167]].

<<page_232.html>>

<<page_232.html_pg232_fn61>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg232_fn61][61]].
/Māyāmoha/, literally, delusion (/moha/) caused by magic illusion
(/māyā)./

<<page_233.html>>

<<page_233.html_pg233_fn62>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg233_fn62][62]]. Jains
would go about naked and carry a small broom made of peacock feathers,
which they used to brush their paths clean lest they should
inadvertently tread on insects or other small living creatures.

<<page_233.html_pg233_fn63>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg233_fn63][63]]. The

<<page_235.html>>

<<page_235.html_pg235_fn66>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg235_fn66][66]]. Because
their sons and others could eat it for them at home in the village, just

<<page_237.html>>

<<page_237.html_pg237_fn67>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg237_fn67][67]]. ‘Fame of
Viṣņu'. In the /Mahābhārata/, the only indication that Kalkin is an

<<page_237.html_pg237_fn68>>[[#chapter5.html#ipg237_fn68][68]].

aborigines, but here probably denoting any non-Hindus.

<<page_242.html_pg242_fn7>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg242_fn7][7]]. According

<<page_244.html_pg244_fn10>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg244_fn10][10]]. The
Tawny Mountain, sometimes called the Blood-red Mountain, is the name of
a Himalayan peak sacred to the Goddess and of a sacred mountain
(Aruņācala) in South Arcot district.

<<page_254.html>>

<<page_254.html_pg254_fn23>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg254_fn23][23]]. This
sentence depends upon a pun: /sneha/ can mean either ‘affection' or
‘oil', the latter the opposite of ashes.

<<page_254.html_pg254_fn24>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg254_fn24][24]]. ‘The
little hero', a name of Nandin, the guardian of Śiva's door.

<<page_258.html>>

<<page_258.html_pg258_fn25>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg258_fn25][25]]. This may
be a reference to one South Indian myth of Gaņeśa's birth: one day
Pārvatī saw the sacred syllable ‘Om', and her glance transformed it into
two coupling elephants, who gave birth to Gaņeśa and then resumed the
form of the syllable ‘Om'. Thus ‘Om' is Gaņeśa's syllable, and the rock
might be marked with this sign.

<<page_258.html_pg258_fn26>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg258_fn26][26]]. That is,
to commit suicide or ‘suttee'.

<<page_259.html>>

<<page_259.html_pg259_fn27>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg259_fn27][27]]. A
reference to the androgynous form of Śiva--Pārvatī.

<<page_259.html_pg259_fn28>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg259_fn28][28]].
Untouchables carry bells to warn approaching members of the higher
castes to avoid them. Śiva as the beggar who wanders through the Pine
Forest often has a bell tied to his leg (see above, myth 39, pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_141][141--4]]).

<<page_259.html_pg259_fn29>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg259_fn29][29]]. ‘The
Sheath' -- that is, the outer layer of the Goddess.

<<page_260.html>>

<<page_260.html_pg260_fn30>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg260_fn30][30]]. As
Nandin is called Śailādi, two etymologies are offered here: that his
mother was a stone (/śilā/), or that his father was a man named Śilāda
(from which Śailādi would be the patronymic).

<<page_262.html>>

<<page_262.html_pg262_fn31>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg262_fn31][31]]. The name
of a narrator of this Purāņa.

<<page_263.html>>

<<page_263.html_pg263_fn32>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg263_fn32][32]]. The
oblations to the ancestors must be offered by a male heir.

<<page_265.html>>

<<page_265.html_pg265_fn33>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg265_fn33][33]]. It is
traditional to inspect a new born child to discover auspicious or
inauspicious signs indicative of his horoscope and fate.

<<page_265.html_pg265_fn34>>[[#chapter6.html#ipg265_fn34][34]]. The
planet of suicides is Saturn, Śanaiścara (‘He who moves slowly'). In
other versions of the myth of Gaņeśa's birth, all of the gods but Saturn
look at the child until, at Pārvatī's importunity, Saturn allows his
gaze to fall upon Gaņeśa, who is thereby beheaded. In this version,
however, it is obviously Śiva's evil eye that beheads Gaņeśa, though the
story-teller is unwilling to state this bluntly and makes an awkward
attempt to place the blame upon Saturn.

<<page_266.html>>

impure position makes him susceptible, as it makes Diti (myth 27, pp.

<<page_288.html_pg288_fn17>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg288_fn17][17]]. Devayānī
and Kaca are in fact more closely related than she will admit, for Śukra
is the son of Bhṛgu, who is, like Aṅgiras, a son of Prajāpati; thus
Devayānī and Kaca have the same paternal great-grandfather.

<<page_288.html_pg288_fn18>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg288_fn18][18]]. Kaca is
twice-born because he is a brahmin, but he is also twice-born because of
his rebirth after the demons have killed him.

<<page_288.html_pg288_fn19>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg288_fn19][19]]. A
reference to the three original goals of life.

<<page_289.html>>

<<page_289.html_pg289_fn20>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg289_fn20][20]]. See
above, myth 24, p. [[#chapter2.html#page_75][75]].

<<page_289.html_pg289_fn21>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg289_fn21][21]]. Compare
the restorations in myths 36, pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_124][124--5]],
and 69, PP.266--8, above, and the revival through an act of truth in
myth 56, pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_201][201--3]], above.

<<page_289.html_pg289_fn22>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg289_fn22][22]]. See
above, myth 67, pp. [[#chapter6.html#page_250][250--51]].

<<page_289.html_pg289_fn23>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg289_fn23][23]]. See
above, myth 62, pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_232][232--5]].

<<page_290.html>>

<<page_290.html_pg290_fn24>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg290_fn24][24]].
According to another version of this story, in the /Matsya Purāņa/, it
is Śukra himself who leaves the demons and goes over to the gods (as
many demons defect), though he returns to the demons immediately.

<<page_291.html>>

<<page_291.html_pg291_fn25>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg291_fn25][25]]. This may
refer to one of the frequent instances in which Brahmā is forced to
promise that a particularly powerful demon will obtain certain
immunities, as Śukra is about to obtain from Śiva. The other text has a
different reading: ‘The brahmin Kāvya recalled what had happened
before...'

<<page_296.html>>

<<page_296.html_pg296_fn26>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg296_fn26][26]]. The most
notorious heresy in ancient India was that of the Materialists, called
Lokāyatikas here but often referred to as Bārha-spatyas, disciples of
Bṛhaspati, to whom their doctrine was often attributed. In view of
this, it is ironic that the true Bṛhaspati accuses Śukra of teaching
the doctrine that he himself (Bṛhaspati) has invented.

<<page_297.html>>

<<page_297.html_pg297_fn27>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg297_fn27][27]]. Śukra
himself seems to be confusing the boon given by Śiva long ago (when Śva
swallowed him) with the one he has just obtained (when Śukra swallowed
smoke).

<<page_297.html_pg297_fn28>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg297_fn28][28]].
Bṛhaspati, who had been there for the last hundred years.

<<page_298.html>>

<<page_298.html_pg298_fn29>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg298_fn29][29]]. The
demons are to become Jains and Buddhists, who beg for their food.

<<page_298.html_pg298_fn30>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg298_fn30][30]]. To
‘heretics', non-Hindus such as Jains, Buddhists, or Materialists, the
presence of women in Hindu rituals would suggest unchastity, while the
animal sacrifice would violate the Jain and Buddhist injunction against
taking life. These and other arguments that follow are traditional
‘heretic' criticisms of various Hindu sectarian practices.

<<page_298.html_pg298_fn31>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg298_fn31][31]]. A
reference to the androgyne. See above, myth 68, p.
[[#chapter6.html#page_259][259]].

<<page_299.html>>

<<page_299.html_pg299_fn32>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg299_fn32][32]]. Budha is
a king of the lunar dynasty, in no way related to Gautama the Buddha.

<<page_299.html_pg299_fn33>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg299_fn33][33]]. When
Tārā, the wife of Bṛhaspati, was raped by Soma, she gave birth to a son
by Soma, as she herself admitted, but was still restored to her husband
Bṛhaspati. Again, it is ironic that the true Bṛhaspati, in disguise,
tells this story to his own discredit, for a man of honour would have
rejected such a wife, as Rāma rejected Sītā in similar circumstances
(see above, myth 56, pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_198][198--200]]).

<<page_299.html_pg299_fn34>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg299_fn34][34]]. See
above, myth 28, pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_94][94--6]].

<<page_300.html>>

<<page_300.html_pg300_fn35>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg300_fn35][35]]. See
above, myth 62, pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_232][232--5]].

<<page_300.html_pg300_fn36>>[[#chapter7.html#ipg300_fn36][36]]. He
alone, the virtuous demon, resisted corruption by Bṛhaspati.

*IV. Editions and translations of Sanskrit texts*

The reader should be forewarned that different editions and translations
use different numbers to refer to chapters and verses of the same
<<back1_split_001.html_page_304>>work, and some refer to the Sanskrit
names of sub-sections rather than to any number at all. This
bibliography lists the most common Sanskrit titles of sub-sections of
large works, and the secondary references in the bibliographical notes
are to the first edition cited for any particular work or (for all
translated works) to the translation.

*A. ŖG VEDA AND ATHARVA VEDA*

/Ŗg Veda/ with the commentary of Sāyaņa, 6 vols. (London, 1890--92).

Translations: into German: Karl Friedrich Geldner, 3 vols., /HOS/, 33--5
(Cambridge, Massachusetts, 1951); into French: Louis Renou, /Études
Védiques et Pāninéennes/ (Paris, 1955--69); into English; Horace Hayman
Wilson (London, 1866); R. T. H. Griffith (Benares, 1896).

Selection (and other translations): John Muir, /Original Sanskrit
Texts/, 5 vols. (London 1872, reprinted Amsterdam, 1967).

/Atharva Veda/ with the commentary of Sāyaņa (Bombay, 1895).

Translations: Maurice Bloomfield, /SBE/, 42 (Oxford, 1897, reprinted
Delhi, 1964); William Dwight Whitney, /HOS/, 7--8 (Cambridge, 1905).

*B. BRĀHMAŅAS AND UPANIṢADS*

/Aitareya Brāhmaņa/ with the commentary of Sāyaņa, /Bib. Ind./
(Calcutta, 1896).

Translation: Arthur Berriedale Keith, /HOS/, 25 (Cambridge, 1920).

/Bṛahaddevatā/ of Śaunaka, /HOS/, 5 (Cambridge, 1904).

Translation: Arthur Anthony Macdonell, /HOS/, 6 (Cambridge, 1904).

/Gopatha Brāhmaņa/ (Leiden, 1919).

1954).

Translation; H. Oertel, /JAOS/, XVIII-XXVIII (1896--1906); (German) W.
Caland (Amsterdam, 1919).

/Kauṣīttaki Brāhmaņa/ (Wiesbaden, 1968).

Translation: A. B. Keith, /HOS/, 25 (Cambridge, 1920).

/Maitrāyaņī Samhitā/, ed. L. von Schroeder (1881; Wiesbaden, 1970).

<<back1_split_001.html_page_305>>/Nirukta/ of Yāska, edited, and
partially translated, by Lakshman Sarup (Oxford, 1921).

/Śatapatha Brāhmaņa/, Chowkhamba Sanskrit Series, 96 (Benares, 1964).

Translation: Julius Eggeling, /SBE/, 12, 26, 41, 43, 44 (Oxford, 1882,
reprinted New Delhi, 1964).

/Taittirīya Āranyaka/ with the commentary of Sāyaņa, /Bib. Ind./
(Calcutta, 1872).

/Taittirīya Brāhmaņa/ with the commentary of Sāyaņa, /Bib. Ind./
(Calcutta, 1859).

1921, reprinted Madras, 1958).

*C. EPICS*

Translations: Pratap Chandra Roy, 11 vols., 2nd edn (Calcutta,
1927--32); Manmatha Nath Dutt, 7 vols. (Calcutta, 1895--1905); J. A. B.

Names of the books (Parvans): 1. Ādi; 2. Sabhā 3. Vana (orĀraņyaka); 4.

Āśramavāsika; 16. Mausala; 17. Mahāprasthānika; 18. Svargārohaņa.

/Rāmāyaņa/ of Vālmīki (Baroda, 1960--75).

Translation: Hari Prasad Shastri, 3 vols. (London, 1962).

Names of the books (Kāņḍas): 1. Bāla; 2. Āyodhyā; 3. Āraņya; 4.
Kiṣkindhā; 5. Sundara; 6. Yuddha; 7. Uttara.

<<back1_split_001.html_page_306>>The most famous ‘translation' of the
/Rāmāyaņa/ in Iidia is the Hindi poem by Tulsī Dās: /The Holy Lake of
the Acts of Rāma, an English Translation of Tulsī Dās's
Rāmacaritamānasa/, by W. D. P. Hill (London, 1952, reprinted Bombay,
1971); another translation of Tulsī Dās's work is by S. N. Ghosal
(Baroda, 1960). A very free ‘translation' of Vālmīki's /Rāmāyaņa/ may be
seen in Aubrey Menen's satire, /Rāma Retold/ (London, 1954).

BIBLIOGRAPHICAL NOTES

*** Notes to the Introduction
:PROPERTIES:
:CLASS: calibre11
:END:
<<back1_split_002.html_insp1>>[[#frontm1.html#iinsp1][1]]. Cf. Xavier S.
Thani Nayagam, /A Reference Guide to Tamil Studies/ (Kuala Lumpur,
1966). See for example /The Śilappadikaram/, translated by V. R.
Ramachandra Dikshitar (Madras, Oxford, 1939) or by Alain Daniélou (New
York, 1962): R. Dessigane, P. Z. Pattabiramin, and Jean Filliozat, /La
légende des jeux de Çiva a Madurai/ (Pondichéry, 1960): /Les légendes
Çivaites de Kāñcīpuram/ (Pondichéry, 1964): R. Dessigane and P. Z.
Pattabiramin, /La légende de Skanda selon le Kandapurāņam Tamoul et
l'iconographie/ (Pondichéry, 1967): C. Jesudasan and Hepzibah Jesudasan,
/A History of Tamil Literature/ (Calcutta, 1961): V. Kanagasabhai, /The
Great Twin Epics of Tamil/ (Madras, 1956): George Uglow Pope,
/Manimekalai/ (Madras, 1911): William Taylor, /Oriental Historical
Manuscripts/ (Madras, 1835). Many Tamil myths may also be found in
Wilber Theodore Elmore, /Dravidian Gods in Modern Hinduism/ (Nebraska,
1915): Gustav Oppert, /On the Original Inhabitants of Bharatavarsha or
India/ (London, 1893): Henry Whitehead, /Village Gods of South India/
(Oxford, 1921); Bartholomaeus Ziegenbalg, /Genealogy of the South Indian
Gods/ (Amsterdam, 1717, reprinted Madras, 1869).

<<back1_split_002.html_insp2>>[[#frontm1.html#iinsp2][2]]. The works of
Verrier Elwin are the richest source of Indian tribal mythology. See
particularly /Folk-Tales of Mahakoshal/ (Oxford, 1944), /Myths of Middle
India/ (Oxford, 1949), and /Tribal Myths of Orissa/ (Oxford, 1954). See
also Lal Behari Day, /Folk-Tales of Bengal/ (London, 1883); Christoph
von Fürer-Haimendorf, /The Aboriginal Tribes of Hyderabad/ (London, 1943
and 1948); Olivier Herrenschmidt, /Le Cycle de Lingal/ (Paris, 1966); C.
H. Tawney and N. M. Penzer, /The Ocean of Story/, 10 vols. (London,
1924--8); and R. C. Temple, <<back1_split_002.html_page_311>>/The
Legends of the Punjab/, 3 vols. (Bombay and London, 1884--1901).

<<back1_split_002.html_insp3>>[[#frontm1.html#iinsp3][3]]. J. N.
Farquhar, /An Outline of the Religious Literature of India/ (Oxford,
1920, reprinted Delhi, 1967); Jan Gonda, /Die Religionen Indiens/, Vol.
II, /Der jüngere Hinduisnius/ (Stuttgart, 1963); translated into French,
/L'Hindouisme récent/ (Paris, 1965); Rajendra Chandra Hazra, /Studies in
the Purāņic Records on Hindu Rites and Customs/ (University of Dacca,
1948); /Studies in the Upapurāņas/ (University of Calcutta, Vol. I,
1958, Vol. II, 1963); Willibald Kirfel, /Das Purāņa Pañcalakṣaņa/
(Bonn, 1927); /Das Purāņa vom Weltgebäude/ (Bonn, 1954); Frederick Eden
Pargiter, /Ancient Indian Historical Tradition/ (Oxford, 1922, reprinted
Delhi, 1962); /The Purāņa Text of the Dynasties of the Kali Age/
(Oxford, 1913, reprinted Varanasi, 1962); A. D. Pusalker, /Studies in
the Epics and Purāņas of India/ (Bombay, 1955); Moriz Winternitz, /A
History of Indian Literature/, Vol. I, Part 2, /Epics and Purāņas/,
translated by Mrs S. Ketkar (University of Calcutta, 1963).

<<back1_split_002.html_insp4>>[[#frontm1.html#iinsp4][4]]. /Aitareya
Brāhmaņa/ 7.13--19; /Rāmāyaņa/ 1.61--2; /Bhāgavata Purāņa/ 9.7.

1.75--91; /Bhāgavata Purāņa/ 9.18--19; /Matsya Purāņa/ 24--42; /Padma
Purāņa/ 2.72--84; /Viṣņu Purāņa/ 4.9.

/Viṣņu/ 4.4; /Viṣņudharmottara/ 1.18.

<<back1_split_002.html_insp7>>[[#frontm1.html#iinsp7][7]]. /Alambusā
Jātaka/ 523; /Nalinikā Jātaka/ 526; /Mahābhārata/ 3.110--13; /Rāmāyaņa/
1.8--10; /Padma Purāņa, Pātāla Khaņḍa/ 13; /Avadānakalpalatā/ 65.

<<back1_split_002.html_insp8>>[[#frontm1.html#iinsp8][8]]. For recent
discussions of the nature and meaning of mythology in general, see Mary
Barnard, /The Mythmakers/ (Athens, Ohio, 1966); Joseph Campbell, /The
Masks of God/, 3 vols. (New York, 1959--64); Mary Douglas, /Purity and
Danger/ (London, 1966); /Natural Symbols/ (London, 1970; Harmondsworth,
1973); Mircea Eliade, /Cosmos and History, The Myth of the Eternal
Return/ (New York, 1954); /The Sacred and the Profane/ (New York, 1959);
/Images and Symbols/ (London, 1961); /The Two and the One/ (London,
1965); /Myths, Dreams, and Mysteries/ (London, 1968); /Myth and Reality/
(London, 1964); G. S. Kirk, /Myth: Its Meaning and Functions in Ancient
and Other Cultures/ (Cambridge and Berkeley, 1970); John Middleton
(ed.), /Myth and Cosmos, Readings <<back1_split_002.html_page_312>>in
Mythology and Symbolism/ (New York, 1967); Henry A. Murray (ed.), /Myth
and Mythmaking/ (New York, 1960); Thomas A. Sebeok (ed.), /Myth, A
Symposium/ (Bloomington, Indiana, 1958); Alan W. Watts (ed.), /Patterns
of Myth/, 3 vols. (New York, 1963). An introduction to the ritual view
of mythology would include the writings of J. G. Frazer, /The Golden
Bough/, 13 vols. (London, 1915); Jane Ellen Harrison, /Prolegomena to
the Study of Greek Religion/ (Cambridge, 1908); /Themis/ (Cambridge,
1912); /Epilegomena/ (Cambridge, 1921); /Mythology/ (London, 1924); the
last three titles reprinted New York 1962--3; and Lord Raglan, /The
Hero/ (London, 1936); /The Origins of Religion/ (London, 1949). For the
psychoanalytic approach to myth, see C. G. Jung, /Symbols of
Transformation/ (Princeton and London, 1967); /The Archetypes of the
Collective Unconscious/ (Zurich, 1954; reprinted in /Four Archetypes/,
London, 1972, and /Essays on a Science of Mythology/, Princeton, 1969);
Paul Radin, /The Trickster/ (with commentary by C. G. Jung and C.
Kerényi, London, 1956); Otto Rank, /The Myth of the Birth of the Hero/
(New York, 1914; reprinted 1959). For the structuralist school see
Edmund Leach (ed.), /The Structural Study of Myth and Totemism/ (ASA
Monograph 5, London, 1967); /Genesis as Myth and Other Essays/ (London,
1969); Claude Lévi-Strauss, /Mythologiques (Introduction to a Science of
Mythology/): I. /The Raw and the Cooked/ (Paris, 1964; London, 1970);
II. /From Honey to Ashes/ (Paris, 1966; London, 1973); III. /L'Origine
des manières de table/ (Paris, 1968); Pierre Maranda (ed.), /Mythology/
(Harmondsworth, 1972); Pierre and E. K. Köngäs Maranda (eds.),
/Structural Analysis of Oral Tradition/ (University of Pennsylvania,
1971); Vladimir Propp, /Morphology of the Folktale/ (University of
Texas, 1968).

<<back1_split_002.html_insp9>>[[#frontm1.html#iinsp9][9]]. Visual
illustrations may serve to reanimate the myths by helping the reader to
see the gods in their true Indian ambience. Representations of the myths
in sculpture and painting may be found in many of the books cited in the
bibliography (particularly the works of Keith, Moor, Rao, Wilkins, and
Zimmer), as well as in the following: Shakti M. Gupta, /From Daityas to
Devotas in Hindu Mythology/ (Delhi, 1973); Veronica Ions, /Indian
Mythology/ (London, 1967); and J. Hackin (ed.), /Asiatic Mythology/
(London, 1963), pp. [[#chapter3.html#page_100][100--146]].

<<back1_split_002.html_insp10>>[[#frontm1.html#iinsp10][10]]. Claude
Lévi-Strauss, /Structural Anthropology/ (New York, 1963), p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_210][210]].

<<back1_split_002.html_page_313>>*Notes to the Text*

The exact Sanskrit source of each myth is given first, followed by a
list of other versions of the myth in alphabetical order of four groups:
Vedic, Brāhmaņic, Epic (where these exist), and Purāņic. Texts available
in translation are preceded by*, and references to secondary versions
are to translations. For the /Mahābhārata/, references are to the
critical edition, followed by Roy's translation (in brackets). These
lists are by no means exhaustive, but merely indicate a line for further
research. For some myths, interpretations and discussions are listed
after the Sanskrit sources; the reader should also bear in mind the
usefulness of many translators' notes.

*** 1. INCEST: THE FATHER COMMITS INCEST WITH HIS DAUGHTER
:PROPERTIES:
:CLASS: calibre11
:END:
<<back1_split_002.html_ganesh>>[[#chapter1.html#jai][*]]/Ŗg Veda/
10.61.5--7; 1.71.5; 1.71.8; 1.164.33.

Veda/ 3.31.1; 10.61.7; */Atharva Veda/ 10.10.16;
<<back1_split_002.html_ganesh2>>[[#chapter1.html#jai2][*]]/Aitareya
Brāhmaņa/ 13.9--10; */Bṛhaddevatā/ 4.110--11; /Maitrāyaņī Saṃhitā/
4.2.12; /Taittirīya Saṃhitā/ 3.4; 10.3; */Tāņḍya Mahābrāhmaņa/ 8.2.10;
<<back1_split_002.html_ganesh3>>[[#chapter1.html#jai3][*]]/Bhāgavata/

*** 18. YAMA REJECTS YAMī
:PROPERTIES:
:CLASS: calibre11
:END:
/Ŗg Veda/ 10.10.1--14.

Compare: */Atharva Veda/ 18.1.1--16; */Jaiminīya Brāhmaņa/ 1.53; /Mait

*** 62. VIṢŅU BECOMES THE BUDDHA TO DELUDE THE DEMONS
:PROPERTIES:
:CLASS: calibre11
:END:
​*/Viṣņu/ 3.17.9--10, 35--45; 3.18.1--33.

Compare: */Agni/ 16; */Bhāgavata/ 2.7; 10.40; 11.4; */Devībhāgavata/
10.5.13; */Garuḍa/ 1.32; */Liṅga/ 1.71; /Nīlamata Purāņa/ 684--90;
/Saura/ 34; */Śiva/ 2.5.3--6; /Jñānasamhitā/ 21.3--24; /Skanda/
4.1.43--58; /Viṣņudharma/ 25.
