<<back1_split_003.html>>

** Appendix D\\
\\
GLOSSARY AND INDEX OF PROPER NAMES
:PROPERTIES:
:CUSTOM_ID: back1_split_003.html_calibre_pb_3
:CLASS: calibre1
:END:
*(N.B. Many of the etymologies are conjectural.)*

Āḍi, a demon, perhaps originally in the form of an aquatic bird;
sometimes regarded as an incarnation of Vasiṣṭha: pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_251][251--61]].

Āditya, a name of the sun. The Ādityas, sons of Aditi (‘infinity') and
Kaśyapa, are solar gods who shine forth at doomsday. Various lists of
their names are given, but important Ādityas are Mitra, Aryaman, Bhaga,
Varuņa, Aṃśu, Indra, Dakṣa, and Vivasvat. Originally eight, they are
later enumerated as twelve: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_30][30]], 39, 91,
106, 179, 185.

Agastya, ‘mountain-thrower' -- so called because he subdued the Vindhyas
-- a great sage; the star Canopus: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_199][199]].

Agni, god of fire. Among his many forms are the brothers Vaiśvānara (the
digestive fire within all men), Gṛhapati (the household fire),
Yaviṣṭha (‘the youngest', fire when it has just been produced from the
wood), Pāvaka (‘the purifier', particularly the fire of lightning),
Sahaḥsuta (‘son of strength'), and Saucīka (‘needle-sharp'): pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_98][98--115]], and /passim/.

Agnihotra, the oblation offered into fire: p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_33][33]].

Ahalyā, ‘unploughable', adulterous wife of Gautama: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_92][92]], 94--6, 299.

Airāvata, ‘born of the milky ocean', the white elephant, Indra's mount:
pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_54][54]], 103, 111, 114, 242, 266--9, 277.

Amarāvatī, ‘place of the immortals', the city of the gods: pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_240][240]], 266.

Ambikā, ‘Little Mother', a euphemistic epithet of Kālī: p.
[[#chapter4.html#page_148][148]].

Anakadundubhi, a name of Vasudeva: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_217][217]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_340>>Ananta, ‘infinite', the serpent upon
which Viṣņu sleeps: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_153][153]], 190,
221, 275.

Anasūyā, ‘free from envy', name of the wife of Atri: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_53][53--5]], 149.

Andhaka, ‘blind', a demon: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_168][168--74]],
238, 242, 256.

Aṅgiras, a great sage, mediator between gods and men (cf. Greek
/angebs/): pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_26][26]], 30, 51, 74, 86, 107--9,
119, 133, 147, 282--8, 296--7.

Araņi, ‘turning round', a fire-stick; name of a sage: p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_51][51]].

Arbuda, ‘the swelling', name of a sacred Śaiva mountain in the west of
India: pp. [[#chapter6.html#page_260][260--61]].

/Arthaśāstra/, the earliest extant Indian textbook of political science,
attributed to Kauṭilya, the minister of Candragupta Maurya: p.
[[#chapter7.html#page_272][272]].

Aruņācala, ‘tawny mountain', pp. [[#chapter6.html#page_244][244--6]].

Arundhatī, the wife of Vasiṣṭha, personified form of the Morning Star.
She alone of the Pleiades remains the model of conjugal chastity: pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_110][110]], 112, 167.

/aśvattha/, ‘under which horses stand', the sacred fig tree, /Ficus
religiosa/: p. [[#chapter3.html#page_103][103]].

Aśvins, ‘horsemen', the Dioscuroi, horse-gods, twin sons of the sun and
a mare. The physicians of the gods: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_39][39]],
56--61, 66--70, 118--19, 202.

Atharvan, a priest in charge of the worship of fire and Soma: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_56][56--8]], 133.

Atri, ‘devourer', a great sage, a son of Brahmā, author of many Vedic
hymns, husband of Anasūyā: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_51][51]], 54,
147, 195.

Ayāsyas, ‘agile ones', descendants of Aṅgiras: p.
[[#chapter2.html#page_74][74]].

Baka, ‘the crane', a demon killed by Kṛṣņa: p.
[[#chapter6.html#page_256][256]].

Bala, nickname of Balārlma, ‘he who delights in power', elder brother of
Kṛṣņa: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_224][224]].

Bali, ‘offering', a demon, son of Virocana, conquered by Viṣņu as the
dwarf: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_82][82]], 178--9, 240--41.

Balarāma, elder brother of Kṛṣņa: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_205][205]], 209, 211, 218--20, 223--5.

Bhadrakālī, ‘gracious Kālī', an epithet of Durgā: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_229][229]], 251.

Bhaga, ‘dispensing wealth', an Āditya, brother of the dawn: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_116][116--17]], 124--5, 143, 253.

Bharadvāja, ‘bearing swift flight' or ‘sky-lark', one of the seven
sages: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_195][195]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_341>>Bharata, a brother of Rāma, son of the
wicked Kaikeyī but devoted to Rāma: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_200][200]].

Bhava, ‘existence', a form of Rudra: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_31][31]],
122--4, 129--30, 135, 141, etc.

Bhoja, a country (or the inhabitants thereof) near the Vindhya
mountains: pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_206][206--7]].

Bhṛgu, a great sage, son of the first Manu, one of the seven sages.
Father of Śukra: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_30][30]], 51, 149--54,
284--8, 292--5, etc.

Brahmā, the creator: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_25][25--55]], and
/passim/.

Bṛhaspati, ‘lord of sacred speech', originally an epither of Indra,
then an independent deity, the preceptor of the gods. The planet
Jupiter: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_30][30]], 72, 74, 86, 118, 126, 178,
268, 281--300.

Buddha, ‘the enlightened', pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_175][175]], 231--5,
289, 300.

Caitraratha, a grove of Kubera cultivated by Citraratha (‘of bright
chariot'), king of the Gandharvas: p. [[#chapter3.html#page_112][112]].

Cākṣusa, see Manu.

Candramas, the moon. Incarnate as a god and as a sage, son of Atri and
Anasūyā: p. [[#chapter1.html#page_55][55]].

Cāraņas, ‘wanderers', celestial strolling players: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_95][95--6]], 208, 226.

Citra, ‘bright-coloured' or ‘variegated', the name of an elephant: p.
[[#chapter3.html#page_111][111]].

Dadhyañc, ‘sprinkling curdled milk', son of an Atharvan priest: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_56][56--60]], 94, 101, 118, 133.

Dakṣa, ‘dextrous', son of Brahmā, one of the seven sages, father of
Satī, Rohiņī, and other wives of the gods: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_46][46--53]], 105--9, 118--25, 137--8, 211,
250--51, 262, etc.

Dark Blue, see Nīla.

Daśaratha, ‘possessing ten chariots', father of Rāma: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_95][95]], 202--4.

Dasra, ‘accomplishing wonderful deeds', an epithet of one or both of the
Aśvins: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_61][61]], 69.

Dattātreya, a sage, son of Atri and Anasūyā, regarded as an incarnation
of Viṣņu: p. [[#chapter1.html#page_55][55]].

Devakī, mother of Kṛṣņa, daughter of Devaka: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_205][205--13]], 230.

Devayānī, ‘leading to the gods', daughter of Śukra: pp.
[[#frontm1.html#page_20][20]], 282--9.

<<back1_split_003.html_page_342>>Devī, the Goddess, the essential form
of whom Durgā, Pārvatī, Umā, Gaurī, etc. are manifestations: pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_238][238--69]], and /passim/.

Dhanañjaya, ‘wealth-conquering', name of a serpent demon in the
/Mahābhārata/: p. [[#chapter4.html#page_132][132]].

Dhanvantari, ‘moving in a curve', the physician of the gods: p.
[[#chapter7.html#page_277][277]].

/dharma/, the social order, the ideal order of the world, social law;
the way things should be: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_48][48]], 143
(incarnate).

Dharmaketu, ‘having justice for his banner', an epithet of the Buddha:
p. [[#chapter4.html#page_145][145]].

Dhenuka, ‘full of cows', a place of pilgrimage: p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_41][41]].

Dhṛtarāṣṭra, ‘whose empire is firm', name of a serpent king also
called Airāvata: p. [[#chapter4.html#page_132][132]].

Digvāsas, ‘clothed in the sky', i.e. naked: p.
[[#chapter4.html#page_145][145]].

Diti, mother of the demons called Daityas, wife of Kaśyapa. Counterpart
of Aditi: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_91][91--4]], 239, 290, 295.

Doomsday clouds, sometimes enumerated as two (Balāhaka, [‘the crane'],
and Saṃvarta or Samvartaka [‘destroying']), sometimes, as seven (the
original two plus Bhīmanāda [‘frightening roar'], Droņa [‘the bucket'],
Caņḍa [‘fierce'], Vidyutpatāka [‘having lightning for its banner'], and
Śoņa [‘bloodred']: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_132][132]], 138, 145,
183--4.

Door of the Ganges, see Gaṅgādvāra.

Durgā, ‘difficult of access', an epithet of Devī in her fierce aspect:
pp. [[#chapter6.html#page_241][241]], 247.

Durvāsas, ‘badly clad' or ‘naked', an irascible sage, an incarnation of
Śiva: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_55][55]], 156, 250.

Gandharvas, celestial musicians. The /Ŗg Veda/ knows only one Gandharva,
the father of Yama and Yamī, who guards the Soma and gives it to the
gods, and presides over marriages. The post-Vedic class of Gandharvas
have similar qualities and are the husbands of the celestial nymphs
(Apsarases) who dance in Indra's court: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_45][45]], 47, 52--3, 63, 82--4, etc.

Gaņeśa, ‘lord of hosts', the elephant-headed son of Śiva: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_54][54]], 165, 168--9, 255--8, 261--9.

Gaṅgādvāra, ‘door of the Ganges', a town situated where the Ganges
enters the plains: p. [[#chapter4.html#page_120][120]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_343>>Ganges (Gaṅgā), a holy river: pp.
[[#frontm1.html#page_20][20]], 41, 54, 120, 131, 153, 160--61,
167--8, 182.

Garuḍa, ‘the devourer', son of Kaśyapa and Vinatā, enemy of serpents.
Identified with fire and the sun. The bird on whom Viṣņu rides. Garuḍī
is the female bird: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_54][54]], 105, 110--12,
191, 221--8, 232, 251--61.

Gaurī, ‘the golden one', a name of Pārvatī: pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_104][104]], 152, 169--71, 242--5, 255--61.

Gautama, a sage: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_94][94--6]], 143, 147,
195, 244.

Gāyatrī, ‘song', a Vedic metre of three lines of eight syllables,
personified as the wife of Brahmā and mother of the four Vedas.
Sometimes personified as a bird: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_177][177]].

Ghora, ‘horrible', a son of the boar incarnation of Viṣņu: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_189][189--97]].

Govinda, ‘cow-finder', a name of Kṛṣņa: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_154][154]], 191--2, 218, 223, 230.

Grandfather, an epithet of Brahmā or Prajāpati: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_36][36--8]], 41--2, 51, 88--90, etc.

Guha, ‘reared in a secret place', a name of Skanda: pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_110][110]], 114, 165.

Guhyakas, ‘hidden ones', a class of demi-gods who, like the Yakṣas, are
the servants of Kubera: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_70][70]], 119.

/guņas/, the three ‘strands' or qualities of which all matter is
composed in various proportions, interwoven like the strands of a rope:
/sattva/ (goodness or light), /rajas/ (dust, passion or activity) and
/tamas/ (darkness, inertia or heaviness): pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_43][43--5]], 50, 145, 148, 150, 154, 186, 227.

Hanūmat, ‘having large jaws', name of a monkey who helped Rāma win back
Sītā by leaping over the ocean and setting fire to Laṅkā: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_198][198]], 201.

Hara, ‘the remover', a name of Śiva: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_133][133--4]], 165, and /passim/.

Hari, a name of Viṣņu. Also the name of a demon, son of Tāraka: p.
[[#chapter4.html#page_128][128]] (the demon), and /passim/.

Haryaśvas, ‘possessing bay horses', name of the sons of Dakṣa: p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_47][47]].

Heramba, ‘the buffalo', a name of Gaņeśa: p.
[[#chapter6.html#page_268][268]].

Himālaya, ‘abode of snow', a great mount or range, father of Pārvatī:
pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_41][41]], 95, 105, 111, 119, 131, 142, 167--8,
254--5, 260.

Hiraņyakaśipu, ‘having golden garments', name of a great demon,
<<back1_split_003.html_page_344>>twin brother of Hiraņyākṣa and father
of Prahlāda, killed by Viṣņu as the Man-lion: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_170][170]], 207--8.

Hiraņyākṣa, ‘golden-eye', name of a great demon killed by Viṣņu in his
boar incarnation. Also called Hiraņyanetra or Hiraņyalocana: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_170][170--72]].

Hotṛ, the priest who offers the oblation: pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_98][98]], 100.

Hrāda, ‘the roar', name of a serpent demon, son of Hiranyakaśipu: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_232][232]].

Hṛṣīkeśa, ‘lord of the senses', an epithet of Viṣņu: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_182][182]].

Indra, king of the gods, son of Aditi and Kaśyapa: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_56][56--97]], and /passim/.

Jagatī, ‘moving', a Vedic metre of four lines of twelve syllables: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_177][177]].

Jaimini, a celebrated sage and philosopher: pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_262][262]], 269.

Jamadagni, ‘fire-eating', a sage descended from Bhṛgu: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_195][195]].

Jambha, ‘jaws' or ‘eye-tooth', a demon killed by Viṣnu: p.
[[#chapter7.html#page_291][291]].

Janaka, ‘begetting', a king of Videha, father of Sītā: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_198][198--204]].

Jātavedas, ‘knowing all who are born', an epithet of Agni: p.
[[#chapter3.html#page_98][98]].

Jayantī, ‘conquering', a daughter of Indra: pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_294][294--5]].

Jyeṣṭhā, ‘the eldest', the goddess of misfortune: p.
[[#chapter7.html#page_277][277]].

Ka, ‘Who?', an epithet of Brahmā: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_139][139--40]].

Kaca, ‘hair', son of Bṛhaspati: pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_281][281--9]], 296.

Kadamba, a tree, /Nauclea cadamba/, with orange-coloured fragrant
blossoms: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_223][223]].

Kadrū, ‘tawny', a daughter of Dakṣa, wife of Kaśyapa, mother of the
Nāga serpents: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_222][222]].

Kailāsa, a mountain in the Himālaya range, the paradise of Śiva: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_119][119]], 151, 154, 162, 244.

Kākutstha, ‘descendant of Kakutstha' [‘standing on a hump', so named
because in a battle he stood on the hump of Indra who had been changed
into a bull], an epithet of Rāma: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_202][202]].

Kālanemi, ‘rim of the wheel of time', name of a demon killed by Kṛṣņa:
pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_207][207--8]].

Kālapṛṣṭha, ‘black-backed', a serpent: p.
[[#chapter4.html#page_132][132]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_345>>Kālī, ‘the black goddess': pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_104][104]], 114, 159, 205--10, 213, 251--9.

Kali Age, the last and worst of the four ages; the present age: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_43][43]], 49, 145, 235--7

Kalinda, a mountain on which the river Yamunā rises: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_70][70]], 222--3, 229.

Kāliya, ‘black', a snake inhabiting the Yamunā river, subdued by
Kṛṣņa: pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_207][207]], 221--8.

Kalkin, ‘impure' or ‘sinful', the last incarnation of Viṣņu in the Kali
Age: pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_175][175]], 235--7.

/kalpa/, an aeon, a day of Brahmā, one thousand ages, a period of four
thousand, three hundred and twenty million years as mortals count them:
pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_39][39]], 43--6, 49, 53, 59, 140, 145, 186.

Kāma, ‘desire', Eros, god of desire: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_54][54]],
154--62, 242, 262.

Kamalāksa, ‘lotus-eyes', a son of Tāraka: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_127][127--37]].

Kaṃsa, ‘brass', the wicked king of the Bhojas, cousin of Devakī, enemy
of Kṛṣņa. Identified with the demon Kālanemi: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_206][206--15]].

Kanaka, ‘gold', son of the earth and Viṣņu in his boar incarnation: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_189][189--97]].

Karkotaka, ‘sugar-cane', name of one of the principal serpents of the
subterranean hell: p. [[#chapter4.html#page_132][132]].

Kārttikeya, ‘son of the Kṛttikās', a name of Skanda: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_49][49]], 114, 269.

/kāśa/, a grass, /Saccharum spontaneum/, used for mats and roofs: p.
[[#chapter3.html#page_100][100]].

Kaśyapa, sometimes Kāśyapa, ‘tortoise' a great sage, husband of Aditi,
Diti, and twelve other daughters of Dakṣa. One of the seven sages; pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_48][48]], 91--2, 147, 170, 173, 195, 222, 269.

Kātyāyanī, an epithet of Durgā: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_229][229]].

Kauśikī, ‘daughter of a sheath', a name of Durgā; also a river in Bihar:
pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_41][41]], 210, 259.

Kaustubha, a fabulous jewel obtained from the churning of the ocean and
placed on the breast of Viṣņu: p. [[#chapter7.html#page_277][277]].

Kāvya, ‘endowed with the qualities of a seer or poet', a name of Śukra:
pp. [[#chapter7.html#page_281][281--300]].

Keśava, ‘long-haired', an epithet of Viṣņu: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_182][182]], 218.

Keśin, ‘long-haired', the name of a demon; in the /Ŗg Veda/, Keśin is
the name of an unorthodox sage who drinks poison with Rudra; elsewhere,
he is a demon in the form of a horse, killed by Kṛṣņa: p.
[[#chapter3.html#page_106][106]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_346>>Kinnaras, ‘What?-men', demi-gods with
the heads of horses and bodies of men, or the reverse. Also called
Kiṃpuruṣas or Aśvamukhas(‘horse-heads'): pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_107][107]], 275.

Kratu, a son of Brahmā, one of the seven sages; also a personification
of the sacrifice: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_51][51]], 123--5, 145, 147.

Krauñca, ‘the curlew', a mountain in the Himālaya range, situated in the
east, on the north of Assam: p. [[#chapter3.html#page_111][111]].

Kṛṣņa, ‘the dark one', an avatar of Viṣņu: pp.
[[#frontm1.html#page_14][14]], 49--50, 52, 154, 175, 177, 181,
203--31, 240.

Kṛta Age, also called Satya Age (‘the age of truth'), the first and
best of the four ages; the golden age: pp.
[[#frontm1.html#page_13][13]], 43, 49, 131, 142, 145, 149, 183, 236--7.

Kṛttikās, ‘the cutters' or ‘the razors', the wives of the Seven Sages,
the Pleiades (sometimes represented as a razor), mothers of Skanda: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_49][49]], 104--15, 141, 167--8, 187, 205, 230.

Kṣatriyas, nobles or warriors, the second of the four classes: p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_28][28]].

Kubera, lord of wealth, guardian of the north, overlord of the Yakṣas:
pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_69][69--70]], 119, 132, 240--41.

Kumāra, ‘the youth', a name of Skanda: pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_110][110--14]], 269.

Kurus, a country beyond the most northern range of the Himālaya, a
country of everlasting happiness, sometimes identified as the ancient
home of the Aryans: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_58][58]], 67, 69.

/kuśa/ grass, /Poa cynosuroides/, a grass with long pointed stalks, used
in certain religious ceremonies: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_92][92]], 95,
100, 147, 173, 222.

Kusumāmodinī, ‘fragrant with blossoms', a mountain goddess: pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_255][255--8]].

Lakṣmaņa, ‘endowed with lucky signs', younger brother and companion of
Rāma: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_200][200]].

Lakṣmī, ‘good fortune', wife of Viṣņu: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_153][153]], 162--3, 203, 215, 241.

Laṅkā, the island capital of Rāvaņa, later identified with Ceylon: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_198][198]].

/liṅga/, the sacred phallus of Śiva: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_26][26]], 54, 137--54, 195, 201--2, 250--51,
257, 262.

Lokapālas, ‘world-protectors', the four guardians of the quarters (Yama
in the south, Indra in the east, Kubera in the north, Varuņa
<<back1_split_003.html_page_347>>in the west); sometimes enumerated as
eight (the first four, plus Agni in the south-east, Vāyu in the
north-west, Soma in the northeast, and Sūrya in the south-west): pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_62][62]], 70, 132, 184, 202, 247.

Madana, ‘the maddener', a name of Kāma: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_157][157--9]].

Mādhava, ‘descendant of Madhu' or ‘vernal', a name of Kṛṣņa: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_156][156]], 179, 192.

Madhu, ‘spring', a demon killed by Viṣņu (not the same person as the
Madhu who is an ancestor of Kṛṣņa): pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_183][183]], 212, 222.

Mahiṣa, ‘buffalo', a demon: pp. [[#chapter6.html#page_238][238--49]].

Maithilī, ‘princess of Mithilā', an epithet of Sītā. Mithilā was the
capital of Videha, the modern Tirhut: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_198][198--204]].

Malaya, a mountain range on the west of Malabar, the western Ghāts: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_181][181]].

Mānasa, ‘spiritual', a sacred mountain and sacred lake on mount Kailāsa,
to which wild geese migrate every year during the breeding season: pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_105][105--6]], 190--91.

Mandara, ‘dense', a mountain: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_81][81]], 105,
131, 149, 169, 172, 274--9.

Māņḍavya, a sage whom Dharma cursed to be born of a Śūdra woman;
Māņḍavya retaliated by cursing Dharma to be born of a Śūdra woman, and
so Dharma was born as Vidura: p. [[#chapter4.html#page_143][143]].

Maṅkaņaka, a sage: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_173][173--4]].

Manu, progenitor of the human race, who are called human (/mānava/)
after him. There are said to have been fourteen Manus, one in each great
age (/manvantara/), of whom the most famous are Manu Svāyambhūva (son of
Śatarūpā and Svayambhū, the self-created Prajāpati), the first Manu;
Manu Revanta (‘brilliant'), son of Vivasvat and the mare Saṃjñā, the
fifth Manu; Manu Cākṣusa (‘perceptible by the eye'), son of Tvaṣṭṛ,
the sixth Manu; Manu Vaivasvata (son of Vivasvat and Saṃjñā), the
seventh Manu and progenitor of the present race of mankind; Manu Sāvarni
(‘of the same kind' or ‘son of the look alike', i.e. the son of Vivasvat
and the Shadow), eighth Manu and first of the future Manus: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_25][25]], 30, 47, 50, 53, 60, 62, 65--7, 70, 75,
98, 149--50, 179--84.

Marīci, ‘shining mote', one of the seven sages, father of Kaśyapa: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_51][51]], 91--2, 147.

Mārkaņḍeya, a great sage who floated on the cosmic waters after the
<<back1_split_003.html_page_348>>dissolution of the universe until he
was swallowed by Viṣņu, in whose body he saw the universe: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_184][184]], 218.

Mārtāņḍa, ‘born of a lifeless egg', an epithet of the sun, so-called
either because the sun is ‘the bird in the sky' and a bird is ‘born of
an (apparently) lifeless egg', or because when the sun was dwelling in
the womb of his mother, Aditi, she performed such severe asceticism that
her husband, Kaśyapa, feared that the egg in her womb would be
destroyed: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_66][66]], 69.

Maruts, ‘flashing', storm-gods, also called Māruts or Rudras, martial
deities who form the entourage of Indra or of Rudra. The seven Maruts
are named Wind-speed, Wind-force, Wind-destroyer, Wind-circle,
Wind-flame, Wind-seed, and Wind-disc (Vāyuvega, Vāyubala, Vāyuhā,
Vāyumandala, Vāyujvāla, Vāyuretas, and Vāyucakra): pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_30][30--31]], 63, 80, 91--4, 96, 117, 123, 129,
156, 173, 205, 267.

Maya, ‘the maker', architect of the demons (not to be confused with
Māyā, magic illusion, a power of god or an aspect of the Goddess): pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_36][36]], 126--8.

Meru, the golden mountain, /axis mundi/: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_41][41]], 111, 119, 155--7, 190, 206, 247, 274.

Mitra, ‘the friend', a god regarded as the worldly counterpart of the
spiritual Varuņa; later, a sage, son of Vaśiṣṭha: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_63][63]], 98.

Mohinī, ‘the enchantress', a form of Viṣņu: pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_277][277--8]].

Nahuṣa, ‘neighbour', a king, father of Yayāti, who usurped Indra's
throne and ruled heaven until he touched with his foot the sage Agastya,
who cursed him to become a serpent: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_132][132]], 143.

Namuci, ‘not releasing [the heavenly waters; that is, a demon of
drought]'. A demon slain by Indra and the Aśvins; later, slain by Indra
alone with a weapon of foam at twilight, a story then told of Indra and
Vṛtra: pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_179][179]], 300.

Nanda, ‘joy', a cow-herd (/gopa/), head of the village of Gokula, who
adopted Kṛṣņa: pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_209][209--17]], 224--30.

Nandana, ‘gladdening', Indra's garden paradise: p.
[[#chapter6.html#page_242][242]].

Nandin, ‘the happy one', an attendant of Śiva, his door-keeper, or his
bull: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_120][120--21]], 151--2, 157, 172,
254--62, 266--9.

<<back1_split_003.html_page_349>>Nara, ‘the man', the primeval Man
associated with Nārāyaņa: pp. [[#chapter7.html#page_278][278--9]].

Nārada, ‘bringing to man' (?), a son of Brahmā, messenger between gods
and men; a meddlesome sage: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_46][46--53]], 119,
166, 207.

Nārāyaņa, ‘the son of Man', or ‘son of the waters', a name of Viṣņu
(compare the etymology offered in myth 54, pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_186][186--8]]): pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_136][136]], 171, 186--91, 202, 226, 240, 274--9.

Narmadā, ‘pleasure-giving', name of a river, the modern Nerbudda: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_184][184]], 233, 300.

Nāsatya, ‘helpful' (traditionally derived from /‘nāsa'/, the nose,
because of the birth of the Aśvins from the nose of the mare), an
epithet of one or both of the Aśvins: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_61][61]], 69.

Navagvas, ‘going by nine' or ‘nine-fold', name of a mythical family said
to have aided Indra in battle: p. [[#chapter2.html#page_74][74]].

Nīla, ‘dark blue', a mountain immediately north of the central division:
p. [[#chapter5.html#page_187][187]].

Nīpa, ‘low', the /Nauclea cadamba/, a tree with orange-coloured,
fragrant blossoms: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_229][229]].

Padmavatī, ‘the lotus-bearer', a name of Śri or Lakṣmī, wife of Viṣņu:
p. [[#chapter6.html#page_268][268]].

Pāka, ‘childish' or ‘naīve', a demon slain by Indra; pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_72][72]], 78, 80, 85--6, 294.

Pāñcāla, ‘of the five tribes', a Yakṣa, son of Kubera: p.
[[#chapter6.html#page_259][259]].

Paņis, ‘misers', a race of demons or of native inhabitants of India,
enemies of the invading Aryans, who fought them for the treasure that
they guarded: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_71][71--4]], 76, 176, 214, 238.

Parvata, ‘mountain', a sage, companion of Nārada: p.
[[#chapter4.html#page_119][119]].

Pārvatī, ‘daughter of the mountain', daughter of Himālaya, wife of Śiva:
pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_62][62]], 119, 152, 163, 168--73, 205, 238,
251, 256, 261--9.

Paśupati, ‘lord of cattle', an epithet of Rudra-Śiva: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_29][29]], 77, 80, 117, 119--20, 125, 136, 148.

The Pillar (Sthāņu), an epithet of Śiva in his chaste aspect, see
Sthāņu.

Pināka, the name of Śiva's bow: pp. [[#chapter3.html#page_111][111]],
122, 124, 133--4, 170, 256--7, 297.

Piśācas, ‘flesh-eaters', a class of demons: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_45][45]], 47, 110, 119, 142, 279.

Pleiades, see Kṛttikās.

<<back1_split_003.html_page_350>>Pracetas, ‘mindful', an ancient sage,
father of Dakṣa Prācetasa: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_48][48]], 51.

Prahlāda, ‘joyful excitement', a demon, a son of Hiraņyakaśipu and
worshipper of Viṣņu, who appeared in the form of a Man-lion to defend
Prahlāda from the abuse of his father: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_170][170]], 179, 246--7, 291, 296--7, 300--301.

Prajāpati, ‘lord of creatures', an epithet of Brahmā or of any of a
class of primeval creators: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_25][25--55]], and
/passim/.

Prakṛti, Nature, composed of the three strands or /guņas/: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_148][148]], 261.

Pulaha, one of the Seven Sages: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_51][51]], 147.

Pulastya, ‘having smooth hair', one of the Seven Sages: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_51][51]], 147.

Puruṣa, ‘the Man', the primeval male; according to Sāṅkhya philosophy,
the Puruṣa is inert spirit that conjoins with Prakṛti, Nature, to
produce the universe: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_27][27]], 34, 148, 150,
223, 226.

Pūṣan, ‘nourisher', a solar deity, keeper of herds and bringer of
prosperity: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_116][116--18]], 124--5.

Pūtanā, ‘stinking', a demoness regarded as causing certain diseases in
children; she tries to kill Kṛṣņa and is killed by him; also regarded
as one of the Mothers who attends Skanda: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_214][214--18]], 242.

Rāgas and Rāgiņīs, ‘emotions', the particular musical formulas out of
which classical Indian music is composed; usually there are said to be
six or twenty-six Rāgas married to six or thirty-six Rāgiņīs: p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_49][49]].

Rāghava, ‘descendant of Raghu', a name of Rāma: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_200][200--204]].

Raghu, ‘fleet', son of Kakutstha, grandfather of Daśaratha: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_204][204]].

Rāhu, ‘the seizer', name of a demon who is supposed to seize the sun and
moon and thus cause eclipses: p. [[#chapter7.html#page_278][278]]

/rajas/, ‘passion', the second of the three /guņas/ or strands, see
/guņas/.

Rākṣasas, ‘to be guarded against', a class of demons: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_45][45]], 47, 84, 107, 110, 119, 139, 142, 152,
199--203, 215.

Rāma, ‘charming', a king, son of Daśaratha and a descendant of Raghu,
regarded as an incarnation of Viṣņu. Also (no relation to Rāma son of
Daśaratha) Rāma is a nickname of Balarāma, elder brother of Kṛṣņa: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_95][95]], 197--204, 299.

Ramaņaka, ‘delightful', name of an island: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_228][228]].

Rambhā, ‘plantain', a nymph: p. [[#chapter4.html#page_173][173]].

Rati, ‘loveplay' or sexual pleasure, the wife of Kāma: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_156][156--9]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_351>>Rāvaņa, ‘screaming', a demon: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_198][198--204]].

Revanta, ‘brilliant', a son of Vivasvat and Saṃjñā, the fifth Manu: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_69][69--70]].

Rohiņī, ‘red', or ‘a red cow', a constellation, wife of the moon; also
(no relation to the constellation) the wife of Vasudeva, mother of
Balarāma: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_30][30]], 205--11.

Ŗtadhāman, ‘whose abode is truth', a name of Viṣņu: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_202][202]].

Rudra, ‘howler' or ‘ruddy one', the Vedic antecedent of Śiva, a
malevolent god associated with wildness and danger. He is the god of
tempests and father and ruler of the Rudras and Maruts: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_116][116--174]], and /passim/.

Rudras, the hosts of Rudra, originally enumerated as eight (Bhava,
Śarva, Paśupati, Ugra, Mahādeva, Rudra, Iśāna, and Aśani [existence, he
who has arrows, lord of cattle, dread, great god, howler, ruler, and
thunderbolt]), later as eleven (Mahan, Mahātman, Matiman, Bhīṣaņa,
Bhayaṃkara, Ŗtudhvaja, Ūrdhvakeśa, Piṅgalākṣa, Ruci, Śuci, and Rudra
[great, noble, wise, terrifying, frightening, he who has the seasons as
his banner, he whose hair stands up, tawny-eyed, brightness, purity, and
howler]). They are sometimes identified with and sometimes distinguished
from the Maruts: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_39][39]], 50--51, 185, 202.

Śabalāśvas, ‘possessing piebald horses', sons of Dakṣa: p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_48][48]].

Śacī, ‘powerful help', wife of Indra: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_78][78]], 94, 156, 240.

Sādhyas, ‘to be attained', a group of twelve celestial beings who
inhabit the region between sun and earth. Nara and Nārāyaņa are listed
among the Sādhyas: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_27][27--8]], 202.

Śailādi, ‘son of Śilāda' or ‘son of a rock', a name of Nandin: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_157][157]], 260--61.

Śāka, ‘vegetable', name of an island, the sixth of the seven great
continents, called after the teak tree growing there: p.
[[#chapter2.html#page_69][69]].

/śakti/, ‘power', particularly the active power of a deity personified
as his wife. The word may also designate a spear or sword: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_69][69]], 113, 139, 162, 166, 238, 243, 250.

Śakra, ‘powerful', an epithet of Indra: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_77][77--90]], 106--115, etc.

/Sāma Veda/, the Vedic book of verses intended to be chanted: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_28][28]], 133, 186, 298.

<<back1_split_003.html_page_352>>Śambara, a demon thrown down a mountain
and slain by Indra: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_179][179]].

Śambhu, ‘granting welfare', an epithet of Śiva: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_38][38]], 54, 124, 139, 157, 160--68, etc.

/śamī/, ‘toil', a tree (/Prosopis spicigera/) possessing a hard wood
supposed to contain fire; it was used to kindle the sacred fire: pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_103][103]], 235.

Saṃjñā, ‘sign' or ‘name', daughter of Tvaṣṭṛ and wife of the sun:
pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_65][65--70]].

Saṃkarṣaņa, ‘he who draws or ploughs', a name of Balarāma: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_209][209]], 211, 218.

Saṃvarta, ‘rolling up' or ‘destroying', a name of a sage descended from
Aṅgiras: p. [[#chapter4.html#page_147][147]].

Saṃvartaka, ‘destroying', name of one of the doomsday clouds or fires:
p. [[#chapter4.html#page_132][132]].

Śanaiścara, ‘moving slowly', a name of the planet Saturn: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_67][67]], 265.

Sanaka, Sananda, Sanātana, and Sanatkumāra (‘the ancient', ‘joyous',
‘eternal', and ‘eternally a youth'), the four mind-born sons of Brahmā:
pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_50][50]], 186--7.

Śaṅkara, ‘giver of peace', a name of Śiva: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_166][166]], 263, etc.

Sāṅkhya, an ancient Indian philosophy which divides the universe into
inert /puruṣa/ and active /prakṛti/, the latter divided into the three
/guņas/ or strands: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_129][129]], 148, 221.

/śapharī/, a fish, either a bright little fish found in shallow water
(/Cyprinus saphore/) or a large carp that preys on other fish: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_182][182]].

Saptasārasvata, the sacred place on the river Sarasvatī where seven
rivers meet: p. [[#chapter4.html#page_174][174]].

/śarabha/, a fabulous animal said to inhabit the snowy mountains and to
be an incarnation of Śiva: pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_193][193--7]].

Saramā, ‘the fleet one', a female dog, mother of the four-eyed brindled
dogs of Yama: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_62][62]], 71--7, 176, 214, 238.

Saraņyū, ‘nimble', daughter of Tvaṣṭṛ, later called Saṃjñā, q.v.:
pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_60][60--62]], 65--70.

Sarasvatī, ‘flowing', the river that, with the Ganges, replaced the
Sindhu as the most holy river of India. Also the goddess of speech, wife
of Brahmā: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_131][131]], 174, 268.

Śarva, ‘possessing arrows', a name of Rudra: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_69][69]], 136, 139, 252--4.

Śaryaņāvat, ‘reedy', a lake: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_58][58--9]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_353>>Ṣaṣṭhī, ‘the sixth', a divine mother
regarded as a form of Durgā and supposed to protect children; worshipped
on the sixth day after delivery, when the chief danger for a mother and
child is considered to be over: p. [[#chapter1.html#page_49][49]].

Śatarūpā, ‘having a hundred forms', name of the daughter and wife of
Brahmā and the mother of Manu Svāyambhuva; sometimes regarded as the
daughter and wife of Manu Svāyambhuva: p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_50][50]].

Satī, ‘the good woman', daughter of Dakṣa, wife of Śiva: pp.
[[#frontm1.html#page_14][14]], 105, 119, 123, 249--51, 289.

/sattva/, ‘goodness', first of the three strands or /guņas/. See
/guņas/.

Sātvatas, a tribe in which Kṛṣņa was born: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_206][206]], 215, 221.

Satyavatī, ‘truthful', name of a servant of the Goddess, a woman said to
have become the Kauśikī river, in Bihar: p.
[[#chapter6.html#page_244][244]].

Satya Age, ‘the age of truth', a name of the first or Kṛta Age: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_43][43]], 49.

Saubhari, a great sage who, in his old age, married the fifty daughters
of king Māndhātṛ and begat one hundred and fifty sons upon them: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_222][222]].

Sāvarņi, see Manu.

Sāvitṛ, ‘the vivifier', a form of the sun, one of the Ādityas: p.
[[#chapter4.html#page_118][118]].

Sāvitrī, ‘descended from the sun', a name of a daughter of the sun, wife
of Brahmā: p. [[#chapter1.html#page_49][49]].

Self-created (Svayambhū), an epithet of Brahmā: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_46][46]], 50, 54, 88, 148--9, 202, 208.

Seven Sages, mind-born sons of Brahmā, themselves Prajāpatis or primeval
creators, forming the constellation Ursa Major; they are married to the
Kṛttikās or Pleiades. The names of the seven varied in different ages,
but among the most famous are Gautama, Bharadvāja, Viśvāmitra,
Jamadagni, Vasiṣṭha, Kaśyapa, Atri, Marīci, Aṅgiras, Pulaha, Kratu,
Pulastya, Bhṛgu, and Dakṣa: pp. 511, 04--5, 131, 141--9, 167--8, 195.

Siddhas, ‘perfected', a class of semi-divine beings of great purity and
perfection and said to possess the eight supernatural faculties
(/siddhis/: the ability to become infinitely small, light, great, etc.):
pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_95][95]], 102, 119, 123, 136, 173, 186,
208, 226.

Śilāda, ‘eating ears of corn' or ‘rock-giver', name of a man, father of
Nandin in his reincarnation: pp. [[#chapter6.html#page_260][260--61]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_354>>Sindhu, ‘the river', probably the Indus
river in the Punjab: p. [[#chapter4.html#page_131][131]].

Sītā, ‘the furrow', daughter of Janaka, wife of Rāma: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_197][197--204]], 299.

Śiva, ‘auspicious', god of ascetics, of the /liṅga/, and of cosmic
destruction: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_116][116--74]], and /passim/.

Śivā, ‘auspicious woman', wife of Aṅgiras (pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_109][109--10]]); also a name of the Goddess (pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_164][164--5]], 251).

Skanda, the six-headed son of Śiva, also known as Guha, Kārttikeya,
Kumāra, etc.: pp. [[#frontm1.html#page_14][14]], 69, 97, 104--15,
159--68, 205--6, 214, 261.

Smara, ‘memory', an epithet of Kāma: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_159][159]], 192.

Soma, the ambrosial offering to the gods, by which they sustain their
immortality; also a name of the moon, in which the ambrosia is stored;
and sometimes incarnate as a god: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_48][48]],
70, 108, etc.

Śrī, ‘prosperity', a wife of Viṣņu: pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_114][114]], 150, 154, 215, 276--7.

Śrivatsa, ‘favourite of Śrī', a distinctive twist of hair upon the chest
of Viṣņu: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_145][145]], 224.

Sthāņu, ‘the pillar', an epithet of Śiva in his chaste aspect: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_37][37--9]], 129, 134--7.

strands, see /guņas/.

Sudarśana, ‘beautiful to see', a name of the discus of Viṣņu: pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_279][279--80]].

Sugrīva, ‘having a fine neck', a monkey king who, with his army of
monkeys headed by Hanūmat, assisted Rāma in conquering Rāvaņa: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_198][198]].

Sukeśa, ‘having beautiful hair', name of a sage: p.
[[#chapter4.html#page_147][147]].

Śukra, ‘bright' (also ‘seed'), name of the guru of the demons, son of
Bhṛgu, regarded as the planet Venus: pp. [[#frontm1.html#page_20][20]],
126, 281--300.

Sumbha and Nisumbha (sometimes Śumbha and Niśumbha), a pair of demons,
brothers, grandsons of Prahlāda, slain by the Goddess: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_210][210]], 241--2, 259.

Suparņa, ‘having beautiful feathers', an epithet of Garuḍa: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_222][222]], 228.

Supārśva, ‘having beautiful sides', name of a sage: p.
[[#chapter6.html#page_239][239]].

Śuṣņa, ‘hisser' or ‘scorcher', a demon of drought killed by Indra: pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_280][280--81]].

Suvṛtta, ‘well-rounded', the name of one of the three sons of Viṣņu in
his boar avatar: pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_189][189--97]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_355>>Svāhā, ‘hail', the exclamation made in
offering the oblation to the gods; also the name of the oblation itself
or the oblation personified as the wife of Agni: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_33][33]], 99, 105, 109--12.

Svayambhū, ‘self-created', an epithet of Brahmā. See ‘self-created'.

/tamas/, ‘darkness', the third of the three strands. See /guņas/.

Tapatī, ‘heating', a daughter of the sun: p.
[[#chapter2.html#page_67][67]].

Tārā, ‘star', the wife of Bṛhaspati, stolen by Soma and restored to
Bṛhaspati after a great battle: p. [[#chapter7.html#page_299][299]].

Tāraka, ‘saving', a demon slain by Indra and Skanda: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_126][126--7]], 136, 154--6, 163, 259.

Tārakākṣa or Tārākṣa, ‘star-eyed', a demon, son of Tāraka: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_127][127--37]].

Tārkṣya, a mythical being, originally a horse, later a bird identified
with Garuḍa, or (in the /Bhāgavata Purāņa/) called the father of
Garuḍa: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_222][222]].

Triple Peak (Trikūṭa), Rāvaņa's mountain stronghold on the island of
Laṅkā: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_191][191]].

Triśiras, ‘three-headed', a demon killed by Indra: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_60][60]], 70--71, 76--80, 84, 86, 91, 282.

Triṣṭubh, a metre of four lines of eleven syllables each: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_177][177]].

Trita Āptya, ‘the third one of the water', a Vedic deity associated with
the Maruts and Indra, said to have fought with Vṛtra and to reside in
the most remote regions of the world: p. [[#chapter2.html#page_71][71]].

Tvaṣṭṛ, ‘architect', the artisan of the gods, maker of divine
implements: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_56][56--71]], 75, 77--81, 91, 126.

Uccaiḥśravas, ‘long-eared' or ‘neighing loudly', the horse of Indra,
prototype and king of horses: pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_222][222]], 243,
276--7.

Umā, said to be derived from the exclamation, ‘U! Mā! [Oh! Don't]'
uttered by her mother when Umā began to practise asceticism; Umā is the
daughter of Himālaya and Menā; the wife of Śiva: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_120][120]], 129, 155, 159, 192, 257--9.

Upabarhaņa, ‘the pillow', a Gandharva regarded as an incarnation of
Nārada: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_52][52--3]].

Uśanas, ‘intense', a name of Śukra: pp.
[[#chapter7.html#page_282][282--300]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_356>>Vaikuņṭha, ‘related to what is not
blunt', a name of Viṣņu and of Viṣņu's heaven: p.
[[#chapter5.html#page_192][192]].

Vaiśrāvana, ‘widely famed', a name of Kubera: p.
[[#chapter4.html#page_119][119]].

Vaiśvānara, ‘relating to all men', a name of Agni, particularly of the
digestive fire: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_30][30]], 99.

Varuņa, ‘all-enveloping', a Vedic god regarded as hating falsehood, and
inflicting diseases, especially dropsy, upon sinners; later, he is the
god of the waters and guardian of the western quarter: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_30][30]], 63, 70, 84, 97--9, 107, 132, 230, 240.

/vaṣaṭ/, an exclamation uttered by the Hotṛ at the end of the
sacrificial verse, when the oblation is cast into the fire: pp.
[[#chapter3.html#page_99][99]], 133.

Vāsava, ‘of the Vasus [q.v.]', a name of Indra: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_80][80--85]], etc.

Vasiṣṭha or Vaśiṣṭha, ‘most excellent' or ‘most wealthy', one of the
seven sages, husband of Arundhatī, owner of the wishing-cow; pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_51][51]], 108, 147, 195.

Vāstoṣpati, ‘house-protector', name of a deity who presides over the
foundation of a house, identified with Rudra: p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_26][26]].

Vasus, a group of eight gods, whose chief was originally Indra, then
became Viṣņu: the eight original Vasus were the deities of day, water,
the moon, the pole star, wind, fire, dawn, and light; later, the Aśvins,
Ādityas, Rudra, Viṣņu, Śiva, and Kubera were regarded as Vasus. When
the Vasu Dyaus (Sky) stole the magic wishing-cow of Vasiṣṭha,
Vasiṣṭha cursed them all to be reborn on earth, the sons of the sage
Śāntanu and the river Gaṅgā: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_39][39]], 50,
119, 143, 185, 202, 241.

Vāsudeva, ‘son of Vasudeva', a name of Kṛṣņa: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_55][55]], 150, 154, 182, 184, 191--2, 236.

Vasudeva, ‘lord of wealth', father of Kṛṣņa, a descendant of Yadu of
the lunar dynasty (and hence called Yādava): pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_206][206--14]].

Vāsuki, one of the great serpent kings: pp.
[[#chapter4.html#page_131][131]], 275.

Vāyu, the wind and its deity: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_28][28]], 31,
93, 185, 241.

Vibhīṣaņa, ‘terrifying', a brother of Rāvaņa who left Laṅkā to join
Rāma and was installed upon the throne of Laṅkā by Rāma after the death
of Rāvaņa: pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_198][198--200]].

Videha, a country (the modern Tirhut) whose capital was Mithilā and
whose king was Janaka, father of Sītā: pp.
[[#chapter5.html#page_198][198--204]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_357>>Vidyādharas, ‘possessing spells',
supernatural beings dwelling in the Himālaya and possessed of magic
powers: p. [[#chapter4.html#page_119][119]].

Vidyunmālin, ‘garlanded with lightning', name of a demon, son of Tāraka
or of Vipracitti: pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_127][127--37]], 240.

Vinatā, ‘bent' or ‘hunch-backed', a wife of Kaśyapa, daughter of Dakṣa,
mother of Garuḍa and the race of birds in some texts, wife of Garuḍa
in the /Bhāgavata Purāņa/: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_222][222]].

Vindhyas, a low range of hills separating North India from the Deccan:
pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_131][131]], 199, 210, 259.

Vipracitti, ‘sagacious', name of a demon, father of Vidyunmālin and
Rāhu: p. [[#chapter6.html#page_240][240]].

Virabhadra, ‘gracious to heroes', a son or incarnation of Śiva, fierce
and hideous: p. [[#chapter6.html#page_251][251]].

Virāj, ‘ruling afar', the first-born of Brahmā, a primeval creator: p.
[[#chapter1.html#page_27][27]].

Vīraka, ‘little hero', a name of Nandin: pp.
[[#chapter6.html#page_242][242]], 254--61.

Vīraņa, ‘heroic', a primeval creator, father of Asiknī, the wife of
Dakṣa: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_47][47--8]].

Virocana, ‘illuminating', a demon, son of Prahlāda and father of Bali:
pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_179][179]], 207, 291.

Viśākha, ‘branched' or ‘forked', a name of Skanda, or of a son of
Skanda; or a demon, regarded as a form of Skanda, dangerous to children:
p. [[#chapter3.html#page_115][115]].

Viṣņu, ‘the pervader', originally a solar god, then the supreme god:
pp. [[#chapter4.html#page_174][174--237]], and /passim/.

Viśvakarman, ‘all-maker', originally a name of Prajāpati, then of
Tvaṣṭṛ: pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_50][50]], 59, 66--9, 127, 131, 185.

Viśvāmitra, ‘friend of all', a great sage originally born as a
Kṣatriya; vying with Vasiṣṭha for the wishing-cow, he became a
brahmin: pp. [[#chapter3.html#page_113][113]], 147, 195.

Viśvanātha, ‘lord of the universe', a name of Śiva and of his great
shrine in Benares: p. [[#chapter6.html#page_261][261]].

Viśvāvasu, ‘beneficent to all', a Gandharva: p.
[[#chapter4.html#page_119][119]].

Vivasvat, ‘shining forth', ‘brilliant', a name of the sun: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_47][47]], 60--61, 65--70.

Vṛņḍāvana, ‘forest of the chorus of women', a grove near Kṛṣņa's
village in the district of Mathurā: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_223][223]].

<<back1_split_003.html_page_358>>Vṛṣaparvan, ‘strong-jointed', name of
a demon: p. [[#chapter5.html#page_211][211]].

Vṛṣņis, ‘manly', a tribe of Yādavas from whom Kṛṣņa was descended:
p. [[#chapter5.html#page_211][211]].

Vṛtra, ‘restrainer', a great demon slain by Indra: pp.
[[#frontm1.html#page_22][22]], 56--8, 74--91, 104, 118--19, 122, 126,
216, 221, 281, 296.

Yājñavalkya, a great sage to whom the sun revealed the /White Yajur
Veda/: p. [[#chapter1.html#page_34][34]].

/Yajur Veda/, the Veda arranged for use in the sacrifice: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_28][28]], 133.

Yakṣas, a class of semi-divine beings, attendants of Kubera, generally
benevolent but sometimes responsible for demonic possession: pp 45, 47,
84, 107, 119, 123, 139, 207, 259.

Yama, ‘twin', or ‘curb' or ‘bridle', king of the dead, sovereign over
hell and the south, the first man, son of Vivasvat and SaraņyūSaṃjñā:
pp. [[#chapter1.html#page_37][37]], 40, 60--70, 97--101, 132, 157, 172,
208, 240--43.

Yamī, twin sister of Yama: pp. [[#chapter2.html#page_60][60--66]].

Yamunā, a river (now called the Jumna), sometimes identified with Yamī
and regarded as the daughter of the sun. It rises in the Himālaya and
flows for 860 miles to join the Ganges at Allahabad: pp.
[[#chapter2.html#page_65][65--6]], 70, 222--9.

Yaśodā, ‘conferring fame', wife of the cow-herd Nanda, foster-mother of
Kṛṣņa: pp. [[#chapter5.html#page_205][205--25]].

Yayāti, a king of the lunar race, son of Nahuṣa; he married Devayānī,
daughter of Śukra, and Śarmiṣṭhā, daughter of Vṛṣaparvan: p.
[[#frontm1.html#page_20][20]].

/yoni/, ‘womb' or ‘source', particularly the sacred image of the organ
of generation of the Goddess, worshipped together with the /liṅga/: pp.
[[#chapter1.html#page_26][26]], 151, 250--51, 262.

Yuga, an age of the world; there are four: the first (Kṛta Yuga or
Satya Yuga) is the best, the golden age, and the last (Kali Yuga), in
which we live, is the worst: pp. [[#frontm1.html#page_13][13]], 43, 49,
131, 145, 178.
